Chapter Two.
Cut the two vessels behind the ears, known as al-Hususan.
If a severe illness affects the eyes or the chest and medical treatment does not help, the next step in treatment is to cut these two vessels.
If you wish to cut these vessels, you should shave the head of the patient with scissors. Then, probe the area with a rough cloth to make the vessel appear. Next, tighten the neck of the patient with the help of his shirt. Then, look where the pulse beats and its position, which are the two depressed areas behind the ears, keeping in mind that some people may have more hidden ones.
Then, mark them with a pencil. Next, cut them with forceps to the bone, and this can be done by exposing the head. If you prefer, you can insert the forceps from under the vessel and cut it from above. The length of the cut should be about two closed fingers.
If the vein is cut off, the blood comes out in a pulsating manner, rushing forward and flowing continuously. If the artery does not appear by touch, it should be estimated from the ear after three fingerbreadths, then marked with ink and incised to the bone, and that which ought to be done.
You send six veins of blood on medium size; perhaps you will send more or less. All of this according to what you see concerning the strength and fullness of the vein. Then look at the wound; if there is anything of the periosteum remaining on the bone, cut it off.
Lest it become a hot swelling, then tighten the wound with a thread of torn linen cloth, and treat it with ointments until it heals.
The Third Chapter.
Tie the arteries in the cheeks.
If a person has a chronic sinusitis or acute fits due to hot humidity and heat in the cheek muscles or severe and persistent headache, and so forth, and treatment methods of medicine have not been effective, we have found that tying or cauterizing the arteries in the cheeks as described can be efficacious.
The procedure for tying the arteries involves shaving the hair from the cheeks. You should aim for the visible artery in the cheek; its pulsation will be evident to you, and very little of it is hidden except in an individual case.
Or when there is severe cold, if it is hidden from you, the patient should raise his neck with the help of a garment. Then you should press the area with a cloth or apply a hot poultice to the spot until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the forceps which is depicted in this figure. Then strip the skin gently with it until you reach the vein. Then insert a probe and draw it upward until you remove it from the skin, freeing it from the sinews beneath on all sides. If the vein is delicate, bend it with the tip of the probe. Then cut a portion of it according to the distance between its ends, and ensure that it constricts without causing bleeding. For if it is not amputated and does not cease, the blood will not stop flowing at all. Then evacuate six ounces to three ounces of blood.
If the vein is large, it should be tied in two places with a doubled strong thread. The thread should either be from linen or from tendon sinew to prevent corruption from reaching it before the wound heals and cause bleeding.
Then cut the remaining portion between the ties. You can do this at that time or after another interval. If you wish, cauterize it with a surgical iron until its ends are amputated. This will serve the same purpose as, or even better than, what we have described.
Except if the patient has a fever or has a disturbed temperament, for cauterization helps in eliminating moisture and is more effective in its benefit. After tying the veins, the area should be stuffed with old cotton and well-packed bandages placed on top. After removing the bandages, it should be treated with drying medicines that promote flesh growth, and by fomentation until Allah wills his recovery.
If bleeding occurs from the vein during your work, act quickly to cut it either by cauterization or by stuffing the area with moist material and tightening your hand until the blood stops. If you have nothing of that sort at hand, place your finger there to constrict the blood flow.
Anoint the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides and it is tightened to what is required, and what is lighter and easier than to ligate the vessel is to cauterize the vein with this two-edged iron poker after learning the places with ink.
And apply it very hot until it reaches the bone and stops the vein at two places so that what is between its ends separates, for it will not stick by this cauterization at all. This is the image of the poker.
It should be sharp like the two small scissor blades, but it should be much less sharp than a scissors blade, for if they were as sharp as scissors, the cold would reach them quickly and the flesh would not stop quickly. If there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire clings to them.
And the flesh cuts quickly, and this work is better than any other and lighter and easier. Let the thickness between the scissors be about the thickness of a finger.
Fourth Section.
On the Treatment of Continuous Flow of Hot Tears into the Eyes.
If the tears are continuous and flow from the veins on the forehead outside, and no treatment with medicines has benefited her, and I have seen the face of the patient turn red. And there is a thrumming in his forehead like the tapping of ants, and his eyes are red and wet, with the eyelids peeling due to the sharpness of the tears, forcing the initial treatment to this work, which they call treating with a sword. It involves ordering the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then make three parallel incisions in the forehead along its length. The length of the incision is about two fingers. Each incision is parallel to the length of the nose at the center of the forehead, the second slightly away from the muscle movement in the temple, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the vessels on either side. Keep your hand away from the connection of the jaws. The distance after each incision should be about three closed fingers. Have with you a prepared sponge slice or many dry porous slices to absorb the blood.
Then insert the sharp forceps whose shape is shown here. From the incision following the temple to the middle incision, strip off all the skin between these two incisions along with the fascia on the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision. Then remove this forceps. And insert another instrument into the first incision, called a sharp scissor from one side and smooth but not sharp from the other. This is its shape:.
And make its sharp side towards the flesh adhering to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone. Push it until it reaches the middle incision, cutting all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do the same in the middle incision to the other one. After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, squeeze the areas where the clotted blood is located. Then in each incision, apply a cotton wick and place it over a pad soaked in a mixture of water, oil, or vinegar and oil.
Lest a hot tumor occur and on the third day the bond dissolves, use much cold water for irrigation, then treat it with basilikons ointment mixed with rose oil and whatever is used to treat wounds until it heals, God willing.

There will be a severe, painful headache deep in the head, followed by continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that such materials and colds arise from deep, numerous blood vessels, and the best treatment for this is this remedy: order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make a single incision in the center of the forehead.
There will be a severe, painful headache deep in the head, followed by continuous sneezing. The incision should go through the bone, and keep your hand away from the movable muscles of the zygomatics near chewing until the bone is exposed and all the blood is suctioned out with the sponge.

Chapter Six.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
All that falls into the ear is of four kinds: either a metallic stone like iron and glass, or a plant seed like chickpeas and nuts, or a liquid thing like water and vinegar.
And if it is an animal, then whenever a stone or a kind of stone that does not exceed in the ear falls into the ear, receive it with the sun-facing ear. If you see the stone, diminish something in it with fat of saffron or myrtle, then try to remove it by moving the head or squeezing with a cloth. Plug the nostrils when a sneeze comes after placing a ring of cloth or wool around the ear and tilt the ear upwards. Often, this treatment will cause it to come out. If it does not come out, try removing it with a gentle cotton swab, which is depicted in this image.
If it comes out with the cotton swab, and if not, try removing it with a gentle curved forceps that has little curvature. If it does not come out with that, make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole with wool.
Plug the area around the tube with a moistened quill softened by fat to prevent any air from entering except through the tube. Then draw it out strongly with your breath. Often, this will cause it to come out. If it does not come out as described, take a small amount of Nabatean gum or prepared gum used for catching birds, and place it at the end of the swab.
After applying a tight cotton pad, then insert it gently into the ear hole after the ear has dried from moisture. If it does not come out with everything as described, proceed to incise before hot swelling or spasm occurs.
The recipe for incising is to open the abscess in the ear first and drain it of blood according to its strength, then situate the abscess before you and turn its top side upwards and make a small incision at the base of the ear where the fat is located in the lower part.
The incision should be crescent-shaped so that you reach the stone, then remove it as much as possible with your instruments.缝合伤口应迅速进行，并彻底处理直至痊愈。

And try to cut that type of fallen seed in the ear with it. Do this only if you are certain that the seed has adhered due to the moisture in the ear, turning it into many small pieces, then remove it with a blind forceps or a gentle probe or by suction.
As we have mentioned, it is easier to remove. If water has entered the ear, it should first be expelled by tickling the nose with a quill or similar object, while stuffing the ear with some old cotton and lying on the side with the ear containing the water facing down; if this removes it.

Chapter Seven.
In the Treatment of Temporary堵塞的耳病。
Some infants may be born with their ears unperforated.
And it may also occur in some people that there is a blockage in the ear canal due to a wound or growth inside it. This blockage may be deep within the ear canal and not visible.
And it may occur slightly outside the ear canal and be visible. That which occurs deep in the ear and is not visible, is most often difficult to remove. And that which is visible should have the ear filled with sunlight. And examine it; if you see the obstruction evident, open it with a gentle forceps; this will be its appearance:
Its tip will have some slight width limited to part of it, while the rest of the forceps is smooth on both sides to avoid injuring the ear. If the obstruction is from flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a gentle clamp and cut it with great delicacy until all the flesh is extracted.
If the obstruction is deep in the ear, take a smooth, gentle probe and warm it slightly in the fire, then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction along with the heaviness of hearing loss that the patient experiences, remove it gently.
And take care not to wound the auricular nerve. Then insert a wick into the ear canal, as large as the opening allows, which you have moistened with Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are safe from the wound joining or take a wick, moisten it in water, and sprinkle it with powdered resin.
And use it; if you see the ear swollen with a hot inflammation, it should be removed and replaced with another wick moistened in cyrtoidium made with rose oil until the hot swelling subsides. Then treat it until it heals.
And if there is a bleeding, wet a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear. Use the remaining treatment to stop the mentioned bleeding as described in the section on cataplasms.
The Eighth Chapter.
In the treatment of warts that occur on the eyelids.
These warts that occur on the eyelids may be wet and may become dry. It should be grasped with forceps or clippers and cut off from its roots. Then apply a paste of powder to the area.
If it bleeds, cauterize it with a gentle iridectomy probe and cauterize it better after cutting. Often it returns if there remains anything from its roots. If you cauterize it with fire, it burns those roots and does not return.
Chapter Nine.
In the treatment of colds affecting the eyelids.
Often something resembling a cold in its firmness occurs on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called a cold, which is an accumulation of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The treatment involves examining it; if the swelling is prominent on the surface of the lid and moves in all directions, then its cure is simple. In this case, make a lateral incision.
Then strip it off from every side gently until it is completely removed. Then clamp it with forceps and cut it out. If you are unable to cut it, do not cut the lid until the incision penetrates through the lid; this will not harm the patient at all. If the incision is large, suture it up and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, you do not need to worry about it, for a ointment will heal and constrict it. If the swelling leans towards the inner surface of the lid, turn the lid inside out, clamp the swelling with forceps without needing an incision, and cut it off from every side.
If the eyelid is excised, this will cause no harm. Then wash the eye after cutting the bridle with salty water and treat the area with a cauterizing agent until the disease is cured.
The Tenth Chapter.
In the treatment of trichiasis that occurs in the eyelid.
Trichiasis is a condition where eyelashes grow inwards towards the eye, affecting mostly boys, making their eyes feel heavy and causing them to be susceptible to nocturnal infections. They are unable to look at sunlight. In order that the tears speedily reach them.
And therefore, you will find them always resting on their faces and sleeping with their faces down. Their eyelids below the eyebrows are wet and show the puffiness of trichiasis. When you press the area with your fingers, you can feel the trichiasis between them. The treatment should be that the patient rests his head on your lap.
Then take a cloth of linen, make a wick from it, and create a circle with that wick as large as the area around the trichiasis on all sides. Place it over the trichiasis and press it with your fingers from all sides to gather the trichiasis in the center of the circle.
Then make a slit in the center of that wetness with the bistoury from side to side, and the slit should not be wider than the incision made in circumcision. As for the depth, you should cut through the skin entirely until you reach the trichiasis.
In most cases, the trichiasis protrudes from the slit in the form of a white fatty piece. Draw it out with a cloth that you have wrapped between your fingers and move your hand to the right and left until it is completely removed. Be careful not to widen the slit too much to avoid the bistoury entering the eye and injuring it.
If the hernia does not appear to you at first incision, it should be necessary to widen the incision slightly gently until the hernia appears, then draw it as we said before. Then immerse a piece in vinegar and water and place it on the spot and tighten it with a bandage. Some people crush salt.
He should place it in the cavity of the incision to dissolve what remains of that moisture, then treat it until it heals. If there is a hot swelling at the spot, treat it with soothing dressings until it heals.
The Twelfth Chapter.
On Various Methods of Tightening the Eye.
If a fold of excess eyelid tissue grows above the natural course under the natural eyelids and becomes constricted, it will harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, drooping of the eyelids, whiteness, and hardness until this becomes a reason for the blindness of the eye.
Tightening the eyelid can be done in four ways: either by cauterization with fire, or by using a sharp medicine as described earlier in the section on cauterization, or by tightening through cutting and stitching, or by using splints, as I have mentioned.
You should place the patient's head on your lap. Then, turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns, do nothing else. Otherwise, insert a needle with a thread underneath the lower eyelid and pass the needle with the thread from above, making sure to keep it near the hairline. Then, draw the thread upwards using the eyelid.
Then turn it with the forceps. Next, incise the inner side of the eyelid without cutting the excess hair with the dissecting forceps from above downward to below. Then draw the thread and place a small piece of cotton or cloth under the eyelid. Finally, suture the eyelid with catgut.
Like the shape of the date leaf, yet it should be such that the shape is in proportion to what you desire for lifting the eyelid, as there may be differences among people. Some may need a sufficient part of the eyelid cut according to how relaxed the eyelid is.
And some may need a lesser cut than all of that, according to the relaxation of the eyelid. Then incise with the forceps along the two lines you taught, starting from the outer margin to the inner margin. Each incision should be near the natural hair by the thickness of a wrinkle.
Then insert the forceps into one of the corners of the skin, peel it completely off, and sew the two lips together with an细针和细线。抹去血迹，将剩余的线缝在眉毛上用一些碎屑固定住。
If you choose to do so, and otherwise I care not, then the stitches and threads should be left in place for about three or four days. Then treat it accordingly. If you prefer to leave the wound unsewn, treat it with something that dries and constricts, as the eyelid will lift upon sealing the wound and bringing it together. Sewing is better.
This method of lifting the eyelid, as mentioned by the ancients, has a burden on the patient; yet it is good work and poses no danger. Another method in lifting the eyelid is to teach the eyelid a shape like that of the date leaf, as we have described. Then lift the eyelid with three forceps, either separated or grouped in this manner:.
Then cut the remaining eyelid with a small scissors in a moderate manner:.
If you cannot hold the probes and they do not remain steady for you, take a needle with a thread and insert it in the middle of the shape and insert another thread near the large opening and a third thread near the small opening. Gather the threads with your fingers moderately, then raise your hand moderately with them.
Lift the taught skin as we described, then suture and treat the wound until it heals. If a hot swelling appears upon cutting or incising, relieve that swelling with calamine or similar anointing medicines.
And it may happen that the lower eyelid turns inward; in which case, what we have mentioned regarding cutting, suturing, and cauterization with fire or sharp medicine should be used. Cautioning by fire or a sharp medicine is more effective and gentler for the patient than cutting and suturing. However, cutting and suturing are more thorough in such cases.
Cauterization with a cautery should be done in this manner: Turn the eyelid inside out and make a cut on the inner side as described. Then, create two thin rods or sticks, their length equal to that of the eyelid and their width less than that of a forceps.
And you should cauterize at the ends of both sides where the threads adhere. Then, gather them gently as is preferable for the eyelid. Tighten the rods from both sides tightly and leave it for days. The tightened skin dies, darkens, and rots.
Until it falls off by itself. If it heals slowly, cauterize it with a cautery, then treat it until it heals. When it adheres, the eyelid will rise and the eyelashes will not fall out. This is the image of the rods, which you should make in this shape and size, both in length and width.
The Twelfth Chapter.
Lifting the Thin Hair from the Eye with the Needle.
If it is a single hair or two hairs, the procedure is to take a fine needle and insert into it a thin smooth silk thread. Gather its ends and tie them in a very gentle knot. The length of the thread should be about a finger's breadth.
Then sew another thin thread onto the needle and insert it into the conjunctiva, and tie its ends if you wish. Then place the patient's head near the sun so that the work can be seen clearly; for this is a delicate procedure. Next, insert the needle with the thread at the root of the excess hair.
And pass it through the eyelid with the clamp until it comes out above the natural hair. Then pull the thread upwards towards the conjunctiva and insert that hair into the conjunctiva if it is one or two, or three at most.
Then pull it with your hand until the hair comes out along with the conjunctiva in the eyelid with the natural hair. If you pulled the conjunctiva but the hair did not come out with it, pull the conjunctiva downward with the thread that you sewed into it until the conjunctiva comes out of the upper punctum.
Then insert the hair back into it and pull both of them. You do this until it rises through the upper punctum, and you will see it clearly with your eye. Then release the conjunctiva and the thread, remove them, and tighten the eyelid, leaving it tightened for two or three days until the hair takes its place and flesh grows on it.
If the hair is short, either add to it a long natural hair which will grow with it, or leave it until it grows longer and then lift it.
The Thirteenth Chapter.
In the Treatment of the Incision That Occurs in the Upper Eyelid.
That incision is called arnopia. This incision can be either natural or accidental. The accidental one occurs from the closure of a wound, a cut, a burn, and so on.
The treatment involves cutting the closure, separating its lips, and inserting a wick of cotton between them. It should then be tied until it heals. One should not use things that dry up and constrict in its treatment.
If you do that, the incision will become worse than before. Instead, use things that relax the tissue, such as hordeum (barley sprouts), fomentation with water in which I have boiled barley and cottonseeds, and ointments of diakiles that have been melted with some fat and applied to the wick.
The principle of its treatment is to strive by every means to prevent it from adhering in the same manner as before. The work we have mentioned is only part of correcting the incision; it is not about restoring the area exactly as it was originally.
The Fourteenth Chapter.
In the Treatment of the Incision That Occurs in the Lower Eyelid.
This shṭarah which is from the lowest is called the true shṭarah and is natural and accidental. The accidental one is from a wound or cut or burn and the like.
The method is to take a needle with a doubled thread and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left wound to the right wound so that the thread is at the ends of the flesh. Then, lift the flesh above with the needle and cut it with a wide forceps.
If the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, otherwise take a merud and place it at the site of the cut. Turn the eyelid with it and make two cuts on the inner side of the eyelid, with the ends of the cuts at the corners of the incision until they meet, forming an angle. Until it meets, then its shape becomes similar to this shape:.
And it is the Greek letter lambda. Then, that flesh is removed to the extent that the sharp side of it is lower than what is below the eyelid. Next, the separate parts are gathered with two needles and sewn with a silk thread. Then, it is treated with the medicines we mentioned for relaxing and suturing until it heals.
And if the shṭarah is accidental, from a cut or suture or burn, it should be incised with a simple incision without penetrating the hairs of the eyelid, as previously mentioned. Then, separate the lips with suturing as we have mentioned.
In treating the shṭarah whether from above or below, the work should be done according to what appears suitable from the form of the shṭarah, for it may differ greatly in appearance. The skilled craftsman finds a way with any means available to him.
Until the form returns to its natural shape or approximates it, and the same disease guides you as to what is suitable for it from work and machine in most cases.
Fifteenth Chapter.
On the Adhesion of the Eyelid to the Mucous Membrane or the Cornea.
This adhesion may affect many people by causing the upper eyelid to adhere to the white of the eye, thus preventing it from moving freely and impairing its natural functions. It should be treated by introducing a moistened wick under the eyelid.
And lift it above or stretch it with forceps, then cut the adhesion with a delicate clamp that is not as sharp as the usual clamps but is slightly dull to avoid alarming the patient during the procedure. The clamp should slip easily without injuring the eye with its sharpness, and your cut should be like peeling off that adhesion.
And expect until the eyelid returns to its natural shape and the adhesion is completely healed. Then, pour saline water into the eye to wash it with it or instill it with a solution of zinc sulfate from a dropper. Next, separate the eyelid from the eye with a cotton wick.
And place over the eye a moistened cloth with egg white. After the third day, use medicated forceps until healed.
Sixteenth Chapter.
On the Cutting of the Osseous Spurs and Necrotic Tissue.
The nail is of two kinds: either it is nervously formed and resembles a soft, thin plate, or it is non-nervous and resembles white dryness that iron touches or which the forceps take, causing it to break.
No forceps were applied in it, and both kinds begin from the upper meatus gradually until they cover the observer and prevent light and eye movement. The work is done by cutting them, placing the patient's head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes, and you lift the eyelid with your hand. Next, you pick up the nail with a fine forceps, extending it upwards. Then you take a needle and insert a hair from a horse's or cow's mane or a strong thread into it, bending the tip of the needle slightly and piercing it through the middle of the nail.
And you pass it through the needle, tying the nail to the thread and extending it upwards. You peel off the side of the nail near the socket as if you were spreading it with the hair until the end. Then you cut the remaining part at the base of the socket with a delicate forceps or a small scissors, leaving the natural nasal lining intact.
To avoid the continuous flow of tears from what is cut, the nail is extended with the forceps alone or with the thread as we have said. Then you peel off the smooth forceps the tip shown in this image.
To prevent the forceps from touching the corneal conjunctiva, which could cause a perforation and injure the socket, then after the cut, you should place something from powdered salt or from colocynth in the eye and leave it for another day. Then treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the nail is non-nervous and unable to receive an needle or hold a splinter, this type of nail is often treated with medications as described in the classification, and if you wish to cut it, open the affected eye.
Take a gentle smooth thin-bladed forceps and apply it to this image:. -remove the nail from above gently, grain it gently. If you see that it is loosening and breaking, only grains of zinger root powder or red incisor root powder or powdered salt on it, and tighten the eye for another day.
Then, redo the procedure until all of it is gone. If you encounter a hot tumor in the eye, leave it and treat the hot tumor until it heals, then redo the graining until it heals. As for any remaining flesh from the cauterization.
If the nodule causes severe pain in the eye, fix that nodule with a splint and cut off some of it without过度处理以免引起眼泪流出，然后滴入凹处的红根或金盏花粉末直至痊愈。
Seventeenth Chapter.
On Cutting the Verruca and What Grows Excessively in the Eye.
It may grow in some people's eyes a red excess flesh overlapping to such an extent that it covers or nearly covers the eyeball, or flows onto the eyelids. Sometimes, the eyelids may turn outward and resemble the varicose vein.
The operation is to place the patient's head in your lap, then open his eyes and grasp all that red flesh with the forceps suitable for this.
Or hold it with forceps or tongs, then cut the first piece after the other until you exhaust all of it by cutting, and preserve from the eye lest it injure it when working. Your cut should be either with the scissors we described for cutting the claws or with a small pair of forceps. As with that which you grasp the spool as shown in the chapter following this chapter.
If your cut is complete and all the red flesh has gone, fill the eye with fine salt or drop in some red cumin or similar edible medicine, and cover the eye externally with a cloth soaked in egg white to prevent hot swelling.
If there remains any red flesh and the blood overcomes you, fearing hot swelling, leave the eye and treat it with what soothes the swelling. Then return to the operation until it heals. Thus do you proceed with the excess flesh that appears in the eye of this kind.
Except that you should avoid treating any weak eye, whether due to nature or appearance, by using iron in the operation. Do not insert your hand into any of these operations until you see that the organ is capable of that work.
The Eighteenth Chapter.
On Lancing Spools from the Eye.
The spools are red veins that weave over the eye, causing obstruction to vision by their presence and weakening the eye with the passage of time. You should first look and see if the eye in which the spools are strong and if there is no other disease except the spools.
Then the procedure is that you direct the patient to place his head on your lap, then suspend those veins with one or two clamps according to your skill, and the clamps should be gentle in this form.
or with two double clamps in one body as shown here:.
Then you gently pick up those veins with a gentle scissors, and wipe the blood now and then until you see the eye; then those veins have disappeared and the blood has coagulated. Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors, and let your work be half a day facing the sun.
And you must be extremely careful in your work lest you cut anything other than those veins. When you are finished, puncture the green or red vein of the eye sharply so that its sharpness absorbs whatever remains of the blood vessels. If you cannot cut all of it in that time, apply a poultice to the eye to alleviate the pain.
And leave it for several days until the pain subsides and the hot swelling is assured. Then repeat the work on this eye in the same manner until it is cured. This is the form of the scissors to alleviate the pain.
And leave it [with the head of the patient on the learner's lap, and another to wipe away the tears and ensure vision in the front and back of the eye]. The principle in the work is to open the eye with clamps or a triangular instrument held by a learner.
Fix your gaze and place the head of the patient on the learner's lap, and another to wipe away the tears and ensure vision in the front and back of the eye. And you have the choice to begin the procedure from the top or bottom of the eye; it is better to clamp two clamps in the upper side, one at the back of the crown and the other at the front.
Hold it between the middle finger and the thumb, then suspend cannulas from opposite sides. Then hold it between the index finger and the ring finger and gently lift your hand until you see all the channels have risen. Cut from the smaller western side if you wish with scissors or with a light forceps.
You should irrigate the wound with a needle and clear all the channels until they rise to the surface of the membrane. Do not cut the suction tube, for tears and blood will mix on you.
Then cut with the scissors what exceeds the coronet from above and below so that nothing remains raw except in the horn. If possible, raise it from the horn using a needle; this is better. It may heal on its own in some people.
If you cut it, it will swell the throat for several days, relieving its pain and reducing the hot swelling. Then resume the treatment on it.
Nineteenth Section.
On Reverting the Feather to the Nose.
The doctors call the feather "nasur." If it is treated with cauterization or with a strong burning medicine as described earlier and does not heal, there is no remedy except to incise the swelling when it is ripe and to extract all the moisture or pus that is in it.
Until the bone becomes apparent. If the bone becomes apparent and you see decay or blackness in it, strip it with an instrument of this kind:
And it is called the coarse head, made from Indian iron. Its head is rounded like a nut and engraved with a delicate engraving either of a spoon or a lancet. You place it on the spot of decay in the bone, then turn it between your fingers while slightly tightening your hand until you know that that decay has been removed.
And do this several times, then apply the dried gripping medications to the area. If the site binds over and flesh grows therein, and the duration comes to an end after forty days without any recurrence and no incident occurs.
Know that it has healed, otherwise there is no remedy except to return the nasal speculum to the nose hole in this manner, which is to expose the bone again with iron or sharp medication. If the bone becomes exposed, take a probe in this form:
The tip of the probe should be triangular, with its apex rounded to a point as you can see. Place it on the bone itself, near the meatus, and keep your hand a little distance from the eye. Then, using the probe, turn your hand until the bone is felt by the patient.
He should find the air escaping from him if he places his hand on his nose. Then, apply the dried gripping medications to the area as I have instructed you. If flesh grows and the place stiffens, the material that used to flow outward will return to the nose, making this easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
In Returning the Eyeball.
If the eye has prolapsed completely and there is no affliction or deficiency in vision, it should be eased first, then detached in the cleft. Then place a probe in the cleft without any difficulty, and extract it gently.
Then place on the eye a splint made from copper, brass, and horn. Tie the eye from above to the bandage with many wrappings. The method of the wrappings is to take soft cloth in small pieces, fold each piece into four folds, and make many such pieces.
Then place one on top of the other as much as the eye requires. Then tie them tightly with a strong bandage and re-suction without necessity. Then remove the suction device and leave the eye tied for a day and a night.
If the bandage relaxes during this period, it should be tightened properly. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, otherwise, reapply the dressing, supports, tightening, and suction until it returns.
The Twenty-First Chapter.
On Cutting the Umbilical Cord.
If a fistula appears in the umbilical tissue, then becomes evident and protrudes outside the eyelid, resembling an umbilical swelling and disfiguring the appearance of the person, and you intend to cut it, the procedure should be as described.
It is to insert a needle into the base of the fistula from bottom to top, then insert another needle with a doubled thread on one side and pass it through. Keep the first needle in its place.
Then cut at the point where the doubled thread bends, tie a portion of the fistula around the needle with this thread, and tighten it properly. Remove the needle and place a damp cloth of egg white over the eye. Leave it until the threads fall off with the fistula, then treat the eye to strengthen it.
Until the wound heals, the fistula should be tightened from all sides with a circle of bandage until the eye swells properly. Then use a fine forceps reaching deep into the eye to release the egg white moisture and reduce the eye, causing it to shrink temporarily. Finally, tighten it until it heals.
The Twenty-Second Chapter.
In the Treatment of Contraction.
This disease, which is called contraction, is a period that gathers in the eye resembling water without any substance. The method to treat this is to place the patient on a chair upright, then take his head with both hands from both sides.
And move it until you see the period rise to the lower part of the eye with your eyes. Then, hold it in place and do not let it move while the light comes out. The patient will see the things as he used to see them. If it does not descend to the lower part, we know it is water. If it does not come down as described, proceed accordingly.
Otherwise, place the patient between your legs. Then take a thin clamp and make a small incision above the corneal membrane near the point of contact between the adherent tissue and the corneal membrane in the fornix until the material comes out.
If it comes out, pour hot water into the eye that has mixed with honey or boiled castor oil and honey. Then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
The Twenty-Third Chapter.
We have mentioned the types of water in the classification and which is suitable for injection as explained and interpreted. Take it from there correctly. Then, you should place the patient between your legs, sitting facing the light near the sun.
And tie his healthy eye tightly and pull it tight, then raise his eyelid with your left hand if the eye with the water is the left eye or with your right hand if the right eye.
Then place the tip of the probe near the crown with a moistened end in the same part of the white of the eye near the smaller punctum. Then push the probe forcefully and turn it with your hand until it enters the white of the eye and feel that the probe has reached a hollow space.
And it should be such that the probe reaches the depth as the distance from the child to the end of the black, which is the crown of the eye. For bronze appears in the same manner to the observer as if he were looking at the eye through a clear corneal membrane, then the probe should be brought upwards to the place where the water is.
Then press it downwards repeatedly. If the water comes out from its socket, then the patient will see what his sight was opened to in its socket and the probe is still in his eye. Then let him rest for a while. If the water rises, lower it again without removing the probe.
Then if it settles and does not rise, remove the probe gently while fumbling with your hand very little by very little. Then dissolve a small amount of pure Anbar salt in water and wash the eye from inside. Then place all around the outside of the eye either ghee or wet wool.
Apply rose oil and egg white and tie the healthy eye with it. As for us and the people of our time, we place on it powdered cumin with egg white, for if the probe does not enter the eye due to its hardness, since there are some whose eyes are exceedingly hard. Fain it is that thou take the clamp which is called the pierid, which is its likeness:
Pierce it with the clamp only through the ligament and do not delve deeply into the piercing; rather, it is that you touch gently upon the urethra and then insert the catheter as we have mentioned. When you are complete in your work, prepare the patient to lie securely asleep on his back in a dark room.
And he should abstain from all movements and coughing. Make his food what softens his nature. Do not move his head to the right or left at all. Let the bandage remain in place until the third day, then remove it from that dark room and test his sight.
Show him things, then replace the bandage on the seventh day. It is not fitting to do this during the treatment or after the insertion from your hour; rather, you should avoid doing so before the water rises quickly due to intense vision.
If a hot swelling appears, it is fitting to examine the eye before the seventh day and treat it with what relieves the swelling. Only then release his sight and place a veil over his face, training his vision from beneath it for days while he remains in that dark room.
He should leave the house gradually and deal with its causes. Know that the insertion is not something the learner can dispense with by merely observing a few times; only then should he proceed to the work. I have been informed that some of the Iraqis mentioned that they make an efficacious insertion in Iraq that absorbs water.
And I have not seen anyone in our land make this, nor have I read about it in any of the books of the ancients. It may be something newly invented. These are images of various types of forceps to help you understand them due to their unfamiliarity.
It is made of brass specially, and its tip is made in this fashion with that particular type, triangular and sharp.
The Fourth and Twenty-fourth.
In the Treatment of Putrefied Flesh in the Nose.
It may grow in the nose various pieces of putrefied flesh, some of which resemble worms with many legs, and some are cancerous, hardened like a clot of blood, and some are soft and not like a clot of blood.
And if the flesh is soft and not malignant or cancerous, it should be placed facing the sun. The nostril should be opened, and the probe inserted into the flesh, then drawn out. The part that has been reached should be cut off with a sharp, gentle forceps from one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone, and if there is something remaining that you cannot cut, remove it gently with one of the delicate instruments until nothing remains. If blood prevails or a hot tumor appears.
Then treat it as appropriate. If it is a malignant tumor, act quickly to incise it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone. Then insert into the nose after cutting, either dust and water or some drink. If the nose opens up, and the moisture drains to the throat.
Then know that it has healed. If the moisture does not drain as it should, know that there is putrefied flesh inside at the top of the loose bones where the instrument did not reach with the cut. At that point, you should take a thick flax thread.
It is tied in many knots and a finger's breadth or less is placed between each knot. The patient devises a trick, inserting the end of the single thread into its nostril with what it can manage after shaping it like a seed, drawing its scent until it reaches the palate.
It is drawn out through the throat. Often, boys in the scribes' circles do such a deed and it is easy for whoever wishes to do it. Then, the two ends of the thread, one that came out through the mouth and the other that remained in the nostril, are brought together.
Then, the flesh is separated by the knots in the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh has been cut by the thread's knots. Remove the thread, which will be found in the nostril after wiping away the blood. Soak it in the Egyptian ointment, and do this for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all that remains of the flesh. Then, in the nostril, a lead pipe persists for days until it heals. If further treatment is needed to dry it up, use this method.
This is a picture of the speculum with which ointments and medicines are poured into the nostril.
It is placed from silver or copper, similar to a small candleholder, open at the top and its channel likewise.
And if you prefer, the pipe is made closed like a reed, and the speculum is flattened.
It has a handle at its end as you can see, which is held when the ointment or any other liquid substance is heated in it.
The Twenty-Fifth Chapter.
On Boils in the Tip of the Nose.
Boils often appear at the tip of the nose and enlarge, increasing in size with time until they disfigure the appearance.
And therefore, it should be cut out at the first appearance and all of it should be excised. Then apply either cauterization or a burning medicine that serves the purpose of cauterization. If it has already enlarged, examine it; if it is hardened and solid like a dark color with little sensation,
If it is hardened and solid like a dark color with little sensation, do not treat it with iron, for it is a cancerous tumor. I have often seen those who had this tumor cut out suffer greatly afterward. If the tumor is soft and bulging but not dark-colored, I have found that cutting it out can be done entirely. Empty the boil and cut it out without hesitation or fear, and treat the area with something that dries and constricts until it heals.
The Twenty-Sixth Chapter.
On Sewing the Nose, Lip, and Ear. If the continuity separates due to a wound or the like, know that when disconnection occurs in any of these membranes, it is rare for work to be effective except in some people.
When such a thing is presented to someone, if the wound is fresh and bleeding, stitch its lips together with缝合线, then treat it until it heals. If the separation of continuity has already separated its lips, making each split independent, it should be necessary to strip each split with a thin layer of its outer skin until it becomes moist.
Then gather the lips with suturing and tighten them, and sprinkle on them the powder of myrrh and frankincense. Place above the sutures a plaster of olive ointment or any other adhesive plaster. Leave it tightened for two or three days, then remove it.
Then change the medicine and leave it until the stitches dissolve of themselves. Then treat her with ointment until it heals. The method of suturing is to gather and separate the contact either by needles as we described in suturing the abdomen, or by thread as we informed you there.
The Twenty-Seventh Chapter.
In the Extraction of Knots That May Affect the Lips.
Many people may develop small, hard tumors within their lips, some resembling chestnut burrs and others varying in size. It should be noted that the lip should be turned and incised at each knot, and the tumor should be removed using forceps.
Then cut it from every side. After cutting, fill the area with powdered honey until the bleeding stops. Rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the areas to contract until the wounds heal.
The Twenty-Eighth Chapter.
On the Cutting of Excess Flesh in the Gum.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gum, which the ancients called apobasis. It should be grasped with forceps or pinched with a clamp and cut at its base. Allow the wound to bleed or let it heal naturally. Then place powdered honey over the area.
or one of the contracted dried vessels, if that flesh returns after treatment, it often returns again. Cut it a second time and cauterize it; for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
In Counting Teeth with Iron.
Foul, rough scales may gather on the surfaces of the teeth from within and without, between the gums. These may turn black, yellow, or green, causing decay in the gums and disfigurement of the teeth.
It is necessary to sit the patient before you and his head on your lap. Extract the tooth and the tooth in which you see the scales and sand-like substance until nothing remains. Similarly, do this for the blackness, greenish color, yellowing, and other such things until you cleanse.
If what is in the first counting is gone, otherwise, re-count it another day and again until you reach your goal. Know that the tooth requires different types of forceps with various shapes and many forms according to what suits your work.
The forceps used to extract a tooth from within are different from those used from outside, and the ones used between teeth have another shape. These are various types of forceps, all prepared for you.
Chapter Thirty.
On Extracting Teeth.
It is necessary to treat the tooth for its pain with every means and delay extracting it if possible, for there is no use in extracting it as it is a noble organ. Yet, if extraction becomes necessary, do not hesitate.
If it is determined that the tooth should be extracted, you must wait until the pain is certain. Often, the pain deceives the patient and he thinks it is in the correct tooth and extracts it. Then, the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted; we have seen this happen repeatedly with cuppers.
If the tooth is certain to be painful, then you must apply force with a forceps around the tooth to loosen the gum on all sides. Then, move it with your fingers or with gentle probes gradually until it becomes loose.
Then, firmly grasp the two main probes with both hands and hold the painful area between your knees to secure it so that it does not move. Pull the tooth straight to avoid breaking it. If it does not come out, use one of those instruments and gently insert it from all sides under the tooth, then move and shake it as you did before.
And if the tooth is perforated or loose, you should fill the hole with a piece of cloth and tightly pack it well with a thin moistened tip to prevent it from breaking while pulling with the probes. You should also probe around the gum on all sides thoroughly.
And preserve your effort so that you do not break it, for if some of it remains, it will cause the patient greater discomfort than his original pain. Be careful not to do as the ignorant cuppers do, rushing to extract it without using what we have described.
Often they cause great misfortunes to people, the lesser of which is that a tooth may break and its root or some of its roots may remain. And if it is removed with some bones of the jaw as we have often witnessed, then he should gargle after its removal with a drink or with salt and vinegar. If bleeding occurs from the place, it often happens in such cases that you should crush a bit of vinegar and apply it to the area.
And if the vinegar does not help, use gentle forceps to move the tooth first. These should be long-tipped and short-cylindrical with a thick handle to prevent them from bending when grasped at the tip on the tooth. This is the image of the larger forceps.
They should be as you see, thick at the handles so that when gripped they do not bend and are long-tipped. They should be made of Indian iron or well-tempered steel with points on their tips that fit into each other for a firm and tight grip.
And the tips can be made like those of a probe, also providing a strong grip.
The third and twentieth chapter.
On the Extraction of Tooth Roots and the Removal of Broken Jawbones.
If a root breaks during the extraction of a tooth, it should be covered with cotton soaked in oil for one or two days until the area relaxes, then the forceps or forceps-like instrument can be inserted. That which resembles its ends the beak of the bird that is called blerage; and this is an image of the forceps.
You have made it like a forceps from within or like a scissor. If this does not work for removal with the forceps, you should dig at the root and expose all the flesh with the probe, then insert the instrument which resembles a small pincers, as shown in this image.
Short at the end, a little thick, and do not make it too thin so that it does not break. If the root comes out with this, otherwise use these other instruments shown here, the first one is triangular at the end with some thickness.

Triangular at the end, bent, with some thickness to prevent it from breaking, and not too thin. Know that instruments for teeth are many, and so are other instruments; they cannot be counted. And a skilled craftsman can invent new ones according to what his work and diseases themselves suggest.
Because there are diseases for which the ancients did not make instruments, since the bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth may break or decay, search for it in its place using one of these instruments and forceps mentioned for extracting the root.
We rely on this with its illustration.

The Thirty-second Chapter.
On the Extraction of Teeth Growing Over Others.
If a tooth grows in an abnormal position, it spoils the appearance of the teeth. This is especially true for women and slaves. You should examine whether the tooth has grown behind another tooth.
If it is not possible to extract or push it back, remove it. If it is attached to another tooth, remove it with this instrument, as shown in its illustration.
And it resembles a small bird's beak. Let it be made of sharp iron, and cut it off in several days due to the hardness of the tooth. To avoid disturbing other teeth, use a refrigerant from India if the tooth is loose and firm. Here is its illustration.
It should be entirely made of Indian metal, with a very fine engraving, similar to the needle refrigerant. Cool the tooth gradually over several days with care to avoid loosening it and causing it to fall out. Then wrap it tightly at the end and remove it with some forceps.
If a tooth has broken and the break causes pain to the tongue when speaking, it should also be cooled to eliminate the sharpness of the break and make it smooth so that it does not hurt the tongue or impair speech.
The Thirty-Third Chapter.

If the front teeth wobble or move due to a blow or fall and the patient is unable to bite on anything to prevent them from falling, treat it with contracting medicines but if the treatment does not work.
The remedy is to tie a thread of gold or silver, with gold being preferable because silver rusts and corrodes after a few days, while gold remains unchanged forever. The thread should be medium in thickness, neither too soft nor too hard, to fit the space between the teeth.
A diagram of the joining process is as follows: Take the thread and introduce its bend between the correct teeth. Then weave the ends of the thread between the moving tooth or teeth until the weaving reaches the correct tooth on the other side.
Then, reweave back to the side where you started. Tighten your thread gently and wisely so that it does not move at all. Secure the thread at the roots of the teeth to prevent it from coming loose. Cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, tie them together, and hide them between the correct tooth and the moving tooth.
To avoid injuring the tongue, then leave it tightened as is for the remainder of the time. If it loosens or breaks, tighten it with another thread so that it can be used for the rest of its duration. This is a diagram of the teeth and the method of joining two correct teeth with two moving teeth, as you can see.
One or both teeth may fall out and return to their place, interlock as we have described, and remain. This is done by a companion who makes a path, and he may carve a piece of cow bone into the shape of a tooth and place it in the spot where the tooth fell out. It is pulled as we have said and remains, providing relief.
The Forty-Third Chapter.
In cutting the ligament that is located under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament that is found under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or it may be accidental, resulting from a wound that healed.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth and place his head on your lap, lift the tongue, then cut that external nerve ligament so that the tongue is released from its constriction. If there is some stiffness and complexity, and this is due to a healed wound, insert a probe and cut the ligament externally until the ligament detaches and the constriction resolves.
Be careful that the cut does not go too deep into the flesh, as this may sever a blood vessel causing bleeding. After cutting, gargle with rose water or vinegar and cold water. Then place a cotton wick under the tongue, which the patient should hold every night.
To prevent it from sticking again, if bleeding occurs, apply a freshly moistened powder to the area. If the bleeding persists, iron the spot with a lens-shaped ironing tool that is suitable for this purpose. Then treat him with all other treatment until he recovers.
The Thirty-Fifth Chapter.
On the Extraction of the Frog Spawn Under the Tongue.
A swelling resembling a small frog may occur under the tongue and prevent it from performing its natural functions, and it might even become a bone to fill the mouth. The treatment for this is to open the patient's mouth towards the sun and examine the swelling.
If you see it pale or dark and hard, and the patient does not feel any sensation in it, do not touch it, as it is a cancer. If it is inclined towards being white with moisture, lance it with a sharp probe and incise it gently, and extract it from all sides.
If the bleeding prevails over you while performing the procedure, apply a poultice of powder until the bleeding stops. Then return to completing your work until you remove it entirely, and gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat him with all other appropriate treatment until he recovers.
The Thirty-Sixth Chapter.
On the Treatment of Swellings in the Tonsils.
And swellings that develop in the throat, like other tumors, may involve glands inside the throat that resemble those found outside and are called tonsils. If these are treated as described in the classification but do not recover,
Then examine it. If the swelling is hard, pale in color, and has little sensation, do not treat it with iron. If it is red in color and its origin is thick, also do not treat it with iron to avoid bleeding. Instead, leave it alone until it matures; either it will dry up or it will burst on its own.
And if it is white in color and round, and its origin is tender, this is what should be cut, and the work therein that you should do is to look before working whether its hot swelling has ceased completely or decreased somewhat.
And then sit the patient with his face towards the sun and his head on your knee and open his mouth and take a spatula between your hands and press his tongue down with this instrument, its image is as follows:.
It is made of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press it on the tongue and see the swelling clearly with your eyes, take forceps and insert it into the gland and draw it out as much as possible without pulling any of the vessels along with it.
Then cut it with an instrument like this, its image is as follows: it resembles scissors but both ends are bent, each one fitting into the other sharply. It is made of Indian iron or Damascus steel:.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with forceps as shown in its image:.
Sharp on one side and not sharp on the other. After cutting one gland, cut the other in the same manner. Then, after cutting, irrigate the patient with cold water or with vinegar and water.
If he experiences bleeding, irrigate with water in which the peel of the pomegranate or the leaves of the laurel or similar astringent substances have been steeped until the bleeding stops. Then treat him until he recovers. And if other swellings appear in the throat besides the glands, cut them as you have been instructed for cutting the glands, equally.
And I treated a woman with a tumor that had grown inside her throat, striking the larynx slightly in its sense, nearly blocking the throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and this had prevented her from eating and drinking water.
And she would have died if she had survived for one or two days. The tumor had grown to produce two branches that protruded through the openings of her nostrils. I acted urgently and inserted a needle into one of them, then pulled it out, extracting a suitable piece with it.
Then I cut it where I encountered it through the nasal opening. I did the same for what protruded through the other nasal opening. Then I opened her mouth, pressed her tongue, and inserted the needle into the tumor itself. I cut a portion of it, and all that came out was a little blood.
Then the woman's throat was cleared, and she immediately began to drink water. She also started eating again. We continued to cut from that tumor repeatedly for a long time, and the tumor would leave a replacement in place of what we cut until this lasted for a long time for both of us.
I treated and cauteryed the tumor inside the throat from above, stopping its growth. Then I left that area and did not know what Allah had done to it after me.
The Seventh and Thirty-First Chapter.
On Cutting a Tumor of the Hyoid Bone.
Which is called Unba if it descends to the hyoid bone and swells, being white and rectangular; if it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called Unba. If treated with what we mentioned in the division and the treatment proves ineffective.
I have seen the hot tumor abate, and it became thin; therefore, it should be cut out. And if it was not joined together in a round manner and did not have length, and was either purple or black or had no sensation, then it should be avoided being cut.
If there is uncertainty about the condition, when you see it as white and long as described, you should place the patient's hand under the solar plexus and press its tongue with the instrument described earlier, then insert the needle into the vein.
You should pull it downward and cut it with one of the two instruments I mentioned for cutting the parotid glands. It is best not to cut more than what exceeds the natural size, for if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
After the cut, use what we described for cutting the parotid glands and treat it until it heals. If the patient is afraid to undergo the cutting, you should apply the alternative method without fear or hesitation. The reason for using this cautery method is with sharp medication; you should place the patient's head on your lap.
Then press its tongue with the instrument we mentioned. Next, take the sharp water described in the section on cautery and mix it with unextinguished ghee, making it neither thick nor thin. Fill the trough of this instrument with it; this is its shape.
Its end where you place the medication should have a trough like that of a spoon. Place the instrument with the medication on the tongue itself, and the patient lying on their side to allow saliva to flow out from inside their throat so that nothing drops down into their throat and hurts them.
Then hold your hand with the remedy and squeeze it on the blister for half an hour until you see it has turned black and the bite of the remedy has calmed down. If you wish, take a cotton cloth and wrap its end around the needle, then dip the cotton in the remedy.
You insert the needle with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton sticks to the blister. Do this repeatedly until you reach what you want for branding the grapevine. Then leave it, as it will wilt and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the remedy, you do so.
After branding, wipe around the grapevine with cotton soaked in oil and dry what is around it from the remedy. Then gargle with cold water and treat externally with poultices and internally with irrigations until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and less fearful.
And the blister can also be treated with what is gentler than branding and cutting. It is treated with incense prepared in this manner: Fustic, Zedoary, Chiretta, Sudobium, Sheh, Bupleurum, Cyperus, and similar herbs from weeds are gathered, either all of them or some of them, in a pot and steeped in vinegar.
And it is boiled with the pot lined with clay of wisdom. There should be a hole in the center of the lid, on which the dried apparatus is placed in this manner: It is made of silver or copper.
Then insert the end containing the reed into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the blister through the tube, causing the blister to rupture. Do this repeatedly until it wilts. Be sure not to prepare this treatment in the early stages of the swelling.
Frequently, it increases in the swelling. Only then should you do so when the hot swelling subsides. If this instrument is not available, take a cane and attach to its end a piece of eggshell so that the patient's mouth does not burn.
Because the eggshell prevents the steam's heat from burning the mouth, this is good for both treatment and safety.
The Thirty-Eighth Chapter.
For the Extraction of Thorns and What May Become Embedded in the Throat.
Frequently, a bone or a thorn or something similar becomes embedded in the throat. You should extract what is visible after pressing the tongue with the instrument when the sun is shining.
Let it be clear to you what is in the throat and what is hidden therein and has not become visible. You should induce vomiting in the patient before his food settles in his stomach, for the embedded object may come out through vomiting or the patient might swallow a piece of rag or a root.
Or he might swallow a piece of dry bread. Alternatively, you should take a piece of sponge that is soft and flexible, tie it in a thread, and have him swallow it. When it reaches the location of the thorn, pull the thread quickly. You should do this several times.
For frequently, the thorn or bone sticks in such a way and can be extracted. If it does not come out as mentioned, then use an instrument made of brass shaped like this:
It should be thicker than the probe slightly, and at its end, there is a hook that the patient inserts into his throat gently while raising his head above. He should take care not to touch his windpipe to avoid causing coughing, and either he pushes the bone or thorn with it or the doctor inserts it by hand.
And the introduction of the probe to her is better for his knowledge of the place of the prolapsed thing, and it drives it downward or draws his hand with the instrument upward; all of this according to what he finds convenient until it is removed.
The Thirty-Ninth Chapter.
On the Removal of the Prolapsed Thing in the Throat.
If the prolapsed thing is treated with the medicine as described in the division of treatment, and if it does not succeed, then examine the probe at the throat after pressing his tongue with the instrument described to you.
If your sight falls on the prolapsed thing, draw it with a small forceps or a fine, tight tweezers. If you are not able to do so, then take a hollow tube and introduce it into the throat near the prolapsed thing. Then insert a heated iron into the hollow tube.
You do this several times. The prolapsed thing should abstain from water all day. Then give him a cup full of cold water and open his mouth wide, allowing him to gargle without swallowing even a drop. Occasionally move the water around with your hand.
If the prolapsed thing drops out when it senses water, then it should be removed as described. If it does not come out, then sprinkle the throat with urine and use the instrument described for burning incense to fumigate the throat. Do this several times; it will drop out.
And the procedure for fumigation is as follows: Take a quantity of embers that have been heated with fire and place it in a pot covered with a lid, making sure there is a hole in the center of the lid. Attach the end of the instrument to this hole. Then throw incense on the embers. Place the prolapsed thing's mouth over the open end of the tube, ensuring that its mouth is closed so that the fumigation does not escape until it is clear that the fumigation has reached its throat.
If the clot falls into place, then it has fallen. If not, incense should be used several times and the patient should endure the thirst, eat salty food and garlic, and not drink water. It must come out through this arrangement. This is an image of the instrument that attracts the clot from the throat when sight falls upon it.
And it resembles the calabash as you see, except that it has this spout which enters the throat, and its ends resemble the beak of a bird. Inside, there is roughening if it touches anything, it will not leave it.
And another instrument to press the tongue. It is a wide ring of iron as large as the mouth will hold above the tongue. It enters the mouth standing on one side, so that the mouth remains open without the patient's willing it, and he cannot dislodge it at all until it is removed.
The description of the instrument by which the mouth is opened in accordance with this same measurement.
Chapter Forty.
It deals with the speech concerning the expansion of tumors and incising them.
Tumors are of many kinds, varied according to what will be mentioned one by one in this book. They differ in their expansion and incision from two perspectives: the first is related to the type of tumor itself and what it contains of moist substances.
The second type is based on the locations where they occur in the body, for a tumor that occurs in the head is different from one that occurs in the neck. A tumor that occurs in muscle tissue is different from one that occurs in a joint. Each has its own treatment.
And some tumors should not be treated until the pus is ripe and complete, and among them are those that should be treated while they are not completely ripe, such as tumors that are near the joints.
Because if a tumor occurs near a joint and takes time to develop, corrupting what is around it, perhaps it will damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, becoming a cause for the disability of that limb or making the tumor close to a vital organ.
Because if you delay treating it until it ripens, you will harm the vital organ. Or it may be near the perineum, so you should treat it intentionally while it is not completely ripe. Only then is it necessary to treat the tumor intentionally while it is not fully ripe, to prevent corruption of the surrounding tissue and from entering the perineum, becoming dangerous.
And it will become, to a certain degree, incurable. You should know the time to treat tumors that are completely ripe, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, fever disappears, redness and swelling diminish, and the head of the tumor becomes soft along with all other signs.
You should locate the puncture at the lowest part of the tumor if possible, to make it easier for the pus to drain downward, or at the most tender part and most tubular. The puncture should be made along the length of the body if the tumors are in areas like the arms and legs.
And for muscle locations, tendon locations, nerve locations, and blood vessel locations, and in general, all flat areas without curvature. As for curved areas, the puncture should be made according to that location. If the tumor is in fleshy areas, it is best to avoid puncturing it.
Until its maturity is complete as we said, if you compress it before that, its pus will remain for a long time and be both abundant and foul. It may even harden its lips and darken its color, and some tumors may become compressed where the body is narrow out of necessity or according to what the organ requires.
It should be compressed with one compression for small tumors and with a wide compression or many incisions according to the size of the tumor. Some tumors may require that the skin be drawn and cut if they have become like rags.
It has reached a point where some have died, as is often seen in many abscesses and hidden infections. Some require an incision with three corners, while others are cut to resemble the shape of a leaf, like the tumor of the liver.
Some require an incision with a circular cut, a crescent-shaped incision, and similar incisions. For those without a head, such as flat, hairless tumors, it should be compressed with a simple compression only. If the tumor is large and has accumulated over a long period of time, it should not be compressed too much to avoid expelling all the pus at once.
But expel some of it, then compress the tumor until another day, and then expel some of the pus as well. You do this gradually until all is expelled.
And especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly frail person. The vital spirit often dissolves suddenly with the expulsion of pus, and the patient may die without you realizing it. Therefore, be extremely cautious with this matter.
After treating these tumors, one should clean the wound and examine it. If the wound of the tumor is small or if there is a single simple incision, use catgut or old cotton for suturing. If the tumor is large and has many incisions, this should be done accordingly.
You should insert a wick into each incision until they reach one another. If part of the skin has been cut from the tumor or drawn in, it should be filled with old cotton or dry catgut and tied for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with ointments as necessary until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a piece of cotton in them and apply it to the bleeding area several times.
If the bleeding continues, you should use the management and treatment, as well as the leeches that we have described in many places of this our book. From the classification, if it is winter and the area of the tumor has many nerves, you should warm the bandages with hot decoction and oil.
You should place them on the area. If it is summer and the areas are fatty, you should apply the bandages soaked in cold water and oil, or in a cold decoction and oil. As we said, by the third day, the tumor should be resolved and cleaned.
You should use what is similar for its treatment until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the detailed methods, I have described each tumor and the way to treat it, organized and summarized.
The Forty-First Chapter.
In切分头皮上的肿块。
Tumors that appear in the scalp are small and belong to the kinds of sloughs contained in follicles, which have conditions resembling a chicken's crop. They are of various types; some are fatty, and some contain moisture resembling mucus.
And some contain moisture resembling soups and stews and the like. Some are hardened and solid. All of them pose no danger in incising and removing them, unless you encounter a vein when incising them.
The procedure for incising them is to probe it first with the instrument whose image will be provided next, which is called the probe, until you know what it contains. If what it contains is moisture, then incise it lengthwise with a simple cut as shown in this illustration.
Begin the incision from line ~b~ to line ~j~. Once the moisture has drained, remove and completely cut the sac that contained it, leaving nothing at all. Often, it returns if even a small part is left.
Then, immerse a cotton pad in the pure ointment if it is ready; otherwise, use salt water. Fill the wound with it and leave it until the next day. It will eat away what remains of the sac. Then, reapply the cotton pad with the Egyptian ointment again, or a third time if needed.
Until you are certain that there is nothing left of the sac. Then, treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the tumor contains a fatty slough, incise it as shown in this illustration.

The Fourth and Forty-Third Chapter.
In cutting into the throat for a swelling that occurs within the throat.
The earlier ones described this cutting into the throat. I have not seen anyone in my country perform it, and this is consistent with their remarks. As for those who have sore throat, it should be avoided to cut into the throat as they do not benefit from it.
To ensure that all the glands and the lung are healthy, and as for those who have a hot swelling in the mouth or throat or the tonsils where it is not due to the trachea, it is necessary to perform cutting into the throat to avoid the corruption that results from suffocation.
If we wish to do this, it should be cut into the throat under three circles of the tracheal rings or four small cuts across between two circles, the size of the cut being in the membrane and not in the cartilage. This place is appropriate for the cut.
Because it is without flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it, if the physician is cowardly it would be appropriate to extend the neck skin with forceps then to cut the skin until reaching the trachea avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.

The Forty-Ninth Chapter.
In cutting the tumor that appears from the artery or vein and is called aneurysm.

The Fifty-Fifth Chapter.
Concerning the tumor that appears from the twisting of the nerve.
A tumor may appear in the nerve if it is struck or subjected to excessive fatigue or something similar. This is more common in the elbows and ankles.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a sensitive tumor whose color resembles that of the rest of the body. It is usually painless, but if pressed with force, the patient feels a sensation like numbness. The tumor does not gather deeply within the body.
But it is beneath the skin and moves in every direction without going forward or backward. If it is in a joint, it should not be treated with iron instruments, as it might cause harm for some time. If it is on the head or the forehead, this does not apply.
Surgical the skin with forceps. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it off at the root. If it is large, clamp it with a forceps, remove it, suture the wound, and treat it until he is cured.
The Fifty-First Chapter.
Concerning the Cutting of Wartles That Appear on the Abdomen.
They may appear often on some people in their abdomens and throughout their bodies. These warts, called fungoid, resemble fungi. Their base is delicate, while their heads are hard. They may grow into small warts or become greatly enlarged.
I have seen a man in whose belly there were two worms that resembled living creatures, without any thin white root. Their lips had changed and cracked, and moisture constantly flowed from them. I cut them out and their weight was about eighteen ounces in one and six ounces in the other.
The work in cutting them is to look and see if the affected part is moist and if the color of the worm is white and wet, with a thin root. Cut it with a broad forceps. Have the cauterizing iron ready in the fire, for often much blood rushes out when they are cut.
If the blood overcomes you, cauterize them. If you see the affected part timid and afraid of being cut with iron, take a tightly wound wire of lead and pull the worm of this kind with it. Leave it for two days, then increase the tension of the lead. Continue to tighten the lead.
The more it affects the worm until it breaks and falls off by itself without effort, if the worm has a thick root, there is some fear and uncertainty in cutting it, especially one found in the belly. But it may be possible to cut half of it or part of it.
Then cauterize it lest it return, and then treat it until the wound heals. Beware that you do not cut a worm that is pale in color, has little sensation, and is unappealing to look at, for it is a cancerous tumor. We will discuss cancer after this.

The Fifty-third Chapter.

The Seventh and Fifty-First Chapter.

And if you cut more skin than necessary and it contracts beyond the proper amount, that will not be greatly harmful either. Treat it as we have described until it heals.
The eighth and fifty-third section.
