Seventh and Eighth Chapter:.
On Amputating the Extremities and Stripping the Bones:.
If the extremities have become infected either from an external cause or from an internal one, if you treat this corruption with medicinal remedies and the treatment is ineffective and you see that the infection is spreading in the limb, nothing deters it.
It is necessary to amputate that limb where the infection has spread, so as to save the healthy part from death or from a calamity greater than the loss of the limb. A sign that this has occurred is when the limb turns black until you think that fire has burned it.
او يعفن بعد السواد حتّى يسعى ذلك العفن الى ما يلى ذلك العضو ويأخذ فى جملة البدن فبادر بقطعه، وكذلك إن كان سبب الفساد عن لسع بعض الهوامّ كعقرب البحر او الأفعى او الرتيلاء ونحو ذلك.
If the corruption or bite is in the tip of the finger, cut off the finger and do not delay the corruption from spreading to the hand. Similarly, if it has taken hold in the hand, cut off the hand at the wrist and do not delay the corruption from spreading until it takes hold in the elbow joint.
If it has taken hold in the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow joint in the same socket. If the corruption is possible and I see that it has spread to near the hip, do not cut off the hip at all, for that would result in the death of a healthy limb. Instead, use other methods of treatment as much as is feasible.
If the corruption has taken hold in the finger, cut off the finger at one of the phalanges. If it has affected the toes, cut off the entire foot. If it has spread to the knee, cut off the thigh at the knee joint.
If the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no remedy but to leave it and allow the healthy part to die. The method of cutting off the limb or preventing its spread is to tie a ligature below the site where you intend to cut and another ligature above the site.
And stretch one ligature downward and another upward. Separate the flesh between the ligatures with a broad forceps until all the flesh is revealed, then cut or spread it. It is best to place linen sutures from all directions.
Lest the saw touch the correct place and cause the patient additional pain and a hot swelling, if bleeding occurs during your work, quickly cover the spot or apply some blood-stopping powders, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured part with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I will give you an example: This symptom occurred in a man's leg, a blackness with a burning sensation like fire.
And that corruption was the first thing to happen in his finger, until it affected all of them. The man saw the corruption spreading in the part with the intense pain and burning he felt, so he had it amputated at the joint, and he recovered.
And when a long time passed, the corruption appeared in his other finger, the middle finger. He came to me, and I treated him with the medicines I applied to the hand after cleaning his body. But the corruption did not subside and began to spread to the other finger.
And the corruption spread to his hand. They took him to have his hand amputated, but I insisted on trying to stop the corruption with medicines, hoping for its subsidence and fearing his death upon amputation due to his weak condition. When he despaired of me, he went back to his hometown.
And I heard that he had his hand amputated, and he recovered. I relate this story to serve as a help for cases of this kind of disease and as a guide upon which to base treatment.
第八十八章
Treatment of Abscesses and the Method of Injecting Them with Medicines.
If a swelling occurs in some fleshy organs and the duration of the swelling lasts for about a day, and then it bursts or enlarges and expels all that was in it of pus, leaving the place empty as if it were a vessel.
And the skin upon which it is like cloth that has become thin and was not severely rotten to affect a bone or a nerve or a ligament; and for this reason, it deserves to be called an abscess and not a carbuncle unless its duration causes corruption to affect any of these organs.
Then it is called a carbuncle or an infection, and its treatment is to cut all of that skin out as soon as possible, especially if it has become thin and resembles cloth, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place due to its corruption.
If you suspect that the skin might adhere and its corruption is not to that extent, and there is still some thickness of flesh in the skin, treat it with injection, which is to look at the abscess; if it is large and the pus it discharges has a foul smell, inject it with medicinal medicine.
Which is a mixture of honey, oil, and honeycomb in equal parts, collected in a container and cooked on fire until the medicine thickens like thick honey. Then take what you need, dissolve it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess. Tie its opening and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
Then dry it out by performing this for several days until the stored discharge is cleansed and the foulness goes away. And if this medicine is not at hand, you may moisten it with water of ashes. Take ashes of vinewood or pine wood and pour water on it.
Then sift it and moisten it with it until you are certain that the stored discharge has been cleansed. If there is no likelihood of the Egyptian medicine or the water of ashes, moisten it with water and honey in which a bit of powdered zanjir has been mixed.
Or moisten it with honey and a drink mixed together, for honey cleanses and washes, while the drink adheres to the stored discharge, especially if the drink has leftover grip and dries up. If you do this several times and are certain that the stored discharge has gone, moisten it with what promotes flesh growth.
Like taking some of the nخلى ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and astringent drink, or moistening it with some other ointments and medicines whose qualities we have established in the article on ointments. If the mouth of the stored discharge is narrow and cannot accommodate the syringe.
Widen it slightly with a iron or insert a triply folded wick in the Egyptian ointment or the scabies ointment until it is wide enough. And if the mouth is also wide, suture its lips and leave enough space so that the syringe can enter without excess.
And if its mouth from which the pus flows is raised above, incise it at its lowest point so that the pus flows downwards. For if the pus becomes lodged in the depth of the stored discharge, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you are unable to incise the abscess downward as desired, it is better to position the part in a way that the pus will flow out easily according to what is available to you and it must not be allowed to become obstructed at all.
And as for the adhesive medications to be placed externally on the abscess and the manner of applying them, you should take one of the adhesive ointments suited to what you want and spread it on a linen cloth. Let the cloth be sufficient to cover the entire abscess. Then, with the scissors, make a hole beside the opening of the abscess slightly wider than the opening of the abscess. Spread a layer of the soft ointment on another linen cloth to cover the hole and place it there, keeping it somewhat distant from the opening of the abscess to facilitate the drainage of pus. Do not remove the large cloth until later.
For several days, while the ointment on the abscess is kept in place, you should remove the one on the opening of the abscess at any time to allow the pus that has accumulated to drain out. You should also observe whether the discharge from it is much or little, whether it is well drained or not.
And in addition to this, examine the location of the abscess itself; whether its owner feels pain there or not, and whether there is a swelling or no swelling. If your treatment continues in this manner and you observe that the material discharging from it is minimal at the opening of the abscess.
Know that the abscess may have become adhered or is close to becoming adhered. In this case, treat it on the following day or by the third day. When the wound heals, replace the cloth with a new one and renew the ointment. If after a long period pus flows from the abscess, do not despair of its adherence.
For its healing is close, for among people some have slow growth of flesh in their wounds and others are the opposite. If it came out from concealment after many days a pus not fully matured, know that the concealment did not adhere. If it delayed adherence, know that the wound is slow to heal.
And if it lasts long, increase in the drying of the medicines. Let the medicines be such that they are wet in their consistency and dry in their strength, like the nakhli ointment if it has been increased with some qalqut (a type of plant). And what is most effective to treat it is to take the nakhli ointment.
And it can be treated with rose ointment. Pour on it the moderate-consistency old drink, then mix it with it and use it. If you do not have nakhli ointment, honey if you cook it until it thickens and use it, or take from rose, patience, and cedar, grind all of them together, and撒布在火上的蜂蜜中。 Then apply it to a cloth and tie it over the concealment, or apply it with honey that you have cooked until it thickens, sprinkle the medications on it, and place a cloth over it and tie it.
And it can be used in such cases as the long zrawnd, the root of the nameconej flower assa-gunnun, and the flour of karkinnah and qantriyun. Use them individually or mix them, grind, sift, and sprinkle on the honey as described and use it. If the concealment lasts and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth.
And it has affected the bones or other solid nerves or ligaments. Cure it with the treatment of naṣūr as described before.
The Ninety-eighth Chapter.
In the Treatment of the Abscess and Pain in the Nail, Cutting off the Excessive Finger, and Separating the Fingers That Are Joined Together.
The abscess is a flesh that grows abundantly under the nail of the thumb or the foot, and it may grow in any finger. If left untreated and its condition worsens, it swells into a hot inflammation.
Or it rots and destroys, eating away the root of the nail, and may even destroy the entire nail. It might extend to the bone, giving off a foul smell, and make the tip of the finger wide, with a discolored appearance. If treated as mentioned in the division and the treatment does not succeed.
It is necessary to cut with iron all the flesh that grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound. For cauterization in this case is highly beneficial. If, however, the bone is sound and the nail is also sound, this may not be necessary.
And if the corner of the nail had pressed the flesh inward, causing it to decay and become infected, it is necessary to place a thin dressing under the corner of the nail that is decaying, raise it above, and cut that flesh gently.
And apply caustic medicines to what remains of the flesh until it is completely gone, then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the decay has affected the bone, it is necessary to cut that bone out and remove it; for nothing will heal while there is a decaying bone in it at all.
If you see decay and corruption setting in at the finger, amputate it during one of the peaceful periods as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail is struck or bruised and severe pain occurs therein, it should be lanced first.
Then lance the nail with a sharp forceps making an oblique incision from above downward, being careful not to reach the flesh beneath the nail, for this will cause severe pain and lead to excessive growth of flesh in that place. Then treat the area with what relieves pain.
As for the extra finger that sometimes develops in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshly, or it may have bones in some parts, and it may also have a nail. The growth of some parts of it may occur at the base of a joint of certain fingers.
Or its growth may occur in some of the phalanges of the finger, which grow in certain phalanges that do not move, and which grow near the joint of the finger; it may move. In such cases, if it is entirely fleshly, cut it off as it is easier to do so by cutting it at its base with a wide forceps.
As for the growth at the base of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so one should avoid cutting it off. As for the growth that occurs in the finger near one of the peaceful periods, it should be cut off first, removing its flesh in a circular manner towards the bone, then exposing the bone with one of those files suitable for this purpose.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the fusion that occurs between some fingers, this often happens and is something that can be born with. It occurs when a wound or fire burn, and the like, causes the flesh to close over it. In such cases, you should lance that fusion.
Until the fingers return to their natural shape, then place between them a needle or prick soaked in rose oil so that it does not adhere quickly.分开它们或在两者之间放一张薄铁片，直到伤口如应地愈合。
And likewise, if the adhesion of some fingers to the palm is exposed, cut that adhesion according to what appears suitable and fits to shape the member.
The Ninety-ninth Chapter.
In cutting the ligaments.
The ligaments are twisted, thick, filled with dark red blood vessels. They occur in most body parts, but most frequently in the legs, particularly in the thighs, calves, and laborers. It should first be treated by vigorously shaking the body from black gall several times.
Then lance the basilicus vein. As for its treatment with iron, it is performed in two ways: one is to cut and remove the black blood, and the other is to incise and completely drain the venous blood. As for cutting, it should be done as follows: first, lance the leg with hot water thoroughly.
Until the thick, dark blood dissolves, then tie the leg of the well from above his thigh to below his knee with a bandage. Then incise the vein in one place or two places or three wide incisions.
Then, squeeze the black blood with your hand from below the leg upwards and from above downwards until you have removed enough blood that you consider sufficient or what the well can bear. Then tie him up and order him to avoid foods that produce black gall. The process is then repeated by vomiting and lancing.
When the veins are filled and this harms the patient, as for leeching it should be done in this manner: if the leg of the patient has much hair, then shave its leg. Then enter him into the bath and wrap his leg with hot water until it turns red and sweat is produced or a strong workout suffices.
If no bath is available to warm the limb, then scrape the skin along the vein either at its top near the knee or at its bottom near the heel. Then open the skin with forceps and strip the vein from all sides until it becomes apparent by touch.
And when it appears, you will see it red and pale. After it is freed from the skin, you will see it like a tendon. Then insert it wet, until it rises and comes out of the skin. Hang it with a smooth blind forceps, then make another incision near that incision about three fingers' width.
Then strip the skin from the vein until it is exposed. Raise it with wet forceps as you did before, and hang it with another forceps as you did first. Make another incision or multiple small incisions if needed.
Then leech it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel. Pull it and leech it until it comes out of the second incision. Then pull it up to the incision above, and do this until you pull it from the third incision all the way up to all the incisions. Cut it when it is fully pulled out.
If it does not respond to pulling and leeching, insert a needle with a strong twisted thread and tie it. Pull it and insert the wet forceps under it, and manipulate your hand in all directions until it comes out, being careful that it does not break. If it breaks, you will find it difficult to leech it thoroughly.
If the ill one is harmed by it, administer it wholly. Place wool soaked in a drink and red ointment or oil on the site of the injuries, and treat until it heals. If the inflammation is contracted with folds and twists towards certain sides, note it.
And if it is not in a straight line as we said, it should be slit along every fold and place of its twist, then tied with tourniquets until it relaxes completely. When cutting, take care not to cut the veins or injure them; otherwise, it may be difficult to dilate it.

Image of the Blind Tourniquet:
It should not have a beak like other tourniquets. It should be blunt at the tip to avoid cutting the vein and should be thick and smooth, as if it were thickened. Otherwise, its thinness would cut the vein with its fineness. Therefore, it should have a thickened protrusion as we said.
The Ninety-Ninth Chapter.
On Dilating the Civil Vein.
This vein develops in the legs in hot countries like Hijaz and Arab lands, and in hot, arid, moderately fertile regions. It may also develop in other parts of the body besides the legs, arising from an infection that occurs beneath the skin.
It occurs within living bodies, maggots, and the gnats of blains, as well as maggots that develop between the skin and flesh. The sign of the onset of this condition is inflammation in the leg followed by a discharge from the affected area, after which the vein begins to emerge from that site.
If it appears to be a plant or an animal, then when its extremity becomes visible, it should be wrapped in a small piece of lead whose weight is from one dirham to two dirhams and tied off, leaving the lead hanging from the stem.
Whenever something comes out, wrap it in the lead and tie it off. If it becomes long, cut off part of it and wrap the rest, but do not cut it from its root before it has all come out, for if you cut it, it will wither and enter the flesh, causing a swelling and a bad ulcer in the place.
Therefore, it should be drawn gradually until it comes out completely, leaving nothing in the body. It may come out from this vein in some people, being as long as five finger-breadths and ten.
And I have heard that it came out to a man for twenty finger-breadths. If it breaks off during your treatment, then insert mercury in its place and dilate it thoroughly with the body until all the material within is expelled, and try to cure the area with medicines for several days.
Then treat him for tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the foot or in the foot itself, causing multiple openings from which branches come out. Treat it as described in the division and as previously mentioned.
The Ninety-Second Chapter.
In the切分线上的虫子，生于皮下，并称为牛病。
This disease is called in some of our countries a sickness of cows because it often affects cows and she is a small single worm that develops between the skin and flesh and moves throughout the whole body ascending and descending.
It is discernible by touch upon its tickling from one part to another until it penetrates wherever it penetrates in the skin a place, and it comes out forming it from corruption of some mixtures as is often the case with worms and blisters of gourds in the belly.
And indeed, one should expect from its harm that when it moves in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it and come out, thereby ruining the eye. This happens often. If you want to treat it and expel it, this can only be done when it tickles and becomes apparent to touch.
It is necessary to tie what is above it and below it with a tight bandage. Then, cut open and remove it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you do not find it, apply heat to the area with fire until it burns it. As we have said, one should expect it to cause harm to the eye to a great extent.
If you see it has gone to the head near the eye, tie tightly under it on the forehead, then cut open and remove it. It is necessary to treat the patient with medicines that purify the body from the corrupt mixtures, and to avoid foods that generate corruption.
The Third Ninety Chapter.
On Cutting for the Disease Known as Nafar.
This disease which we call nafar in our country is a pain that appears in some organs and then transfers from organ to organ, and I have seen it as described to you.
I was called to a sick woman in some wilderness. She uncovered her arm and I perceived a slight pulsation in the vein of the arm's bracelet. After an hour, I saw that pulse moving with the artery as the worm moves up to its elbow, faster than can be imagined, like mercury.
If the pain flows from one place to another, it disappears from that place and settles in the elbow. Then, after a while, it flows throughout the entire body until it reaches the other arm. She told me that her whole body shook as I observed.
I was amazed at the speed of its transfer from one organ to another. Before I saw this disease with my own eyes in such a manner, I had only seen groups of people experiencing pain that transferred from one organ to another, but not as clearly and distinctly as I saw it in this woman.
I did not realize this until I understood that the woman was from the desert, lean-bodied with exposed veins. This is why the transferring pulse was discernible to the touch. It follows that it would not be visible in those who are well-off and have hidden, vein-covered bodies.
If you wish to treat it and the patient feels the pain, it will become visible to the naked eye as we have mentioned. Tighten above and below it with a clamp and cut through it until that heated pulse is removed, and the area is cleansed.
If you do not see it with your own eyes, examine it by fumigating the body and what is meant to dispel winds and散掉它们，例如豌豆子和斯肯宾杰尔子以及类似的一些药物。这里的“散掉它们”似乎不完整或有误，可能是指某种治疗方法或处理方式，请根据上下文补全。请检查并修正以获得准确翻译。
Chapter Forty-Nine.
Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows may differ according to their types and the parts of the body in which they are located. As for the difference by types, some are large and small, some are hollow glass and some are solid glass, and some have three corners and four corners.
And some have tongues and some have shards. As for the difference according to the organs in which they are located, they are of two kinds: either the organs are among the principal hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, and bladder and similar organs. If an arrow is lodged in one of these organs, and you observe the signs of death that I will describe to you later, it should be avoided to extract that arrow from the body, as death usually ensues for the patient in most cases. And if you do not observe these bad signs and the arrow has not pierced deeply into the organ, extract it and treat the wound. As for the signs of the brain, if an arrow strikes it and pierces through the bone and wounds the dura mater covering the brain, then he is exposed to severe headache, vertigo, and dizziness.
And if you do not observe these bad signs and the arrow has not pierced deeply into the organ, extract it and treat the wound. As for the signs of the brain, if an arrow strikes it and pierces through the bone and wounds the dura mater covering the brain, then he is exposed to severe headache, vertigo, and dizziness. And redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, convulsions, confusion of mind, vomiting once or twice. Blood may come out from the nostrils or ears, or speech may be lost with the voice going away. From the wound place, white moisture resembling porridge may come out, flowing like watery meat.
If you see these signs, withhold treatment for the wounded and extraction of the arrow if it has not been removed. As for the signs of the arrow if it struck the heart and is near the left breast and you feel it as though it had penetrated something solid rather than something empty.
Perhaps the arrow has a movement resembling the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound. Following this, there is retraction of the limbs and cold sweat, and fainting occurs. Know that death is certain in such cases. As for the signs of the arrow if it wounds the lung, yellowish-bile blood comes out from the wound.
The vessels that follow the neck swell, and the wound changes color. The breathing is high-pitched, and cold air is sought. If the arrow struck the diaphragm in the chest, it would be close to the smaller ribs, with heavy breathing accompanied by severe pain.
He heaves and all the muscles of the flanks move. If the arrow struck the liver, this was followed by severe pain and blood came out of the wound resembling the color of the liver. If the arrow struck the stomach, something undigested might come out of the wound.
It is evident. If the arrow struck the abdomen and caused it to swell, and something from the feces came out of the wound or a intestine had ruptured, there is no hope for treatment nor for extracting the arrow. If the arrow struck the bladder and urine came out.
Something protruded outward, and the pain intensified for the wounded. Know that he is lost. As for other parts such as the face, eyes, throat, shoulders, arms, spine, vertebras, thighs, legs, and similar parts, it is generally accepted that they remain unaffected.
When the arrow did not hit a vein or nerve and was not poisoned, and I inform you of some of what I have seen concerning these arrows so that you may infer from it a means of treatment, namely that an arrow struck a man in his meqat al-ayni at the root of the nose.
He extracted it for him from the other side under the auricular cartilage and he recovered without anything happening to his eye, and I extracted another arrow from a Jew who was struck by it in the cartilage of his eye under the lower eyelid; the arrow had embedded itself, and I only managed to remove its small point that adhered to the wood.
It was a large arrow from the composite csii arrows, square iron without feathers. It had no fletching. The Jewish man recovered and nothing harmful happened to his eye, and I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian; the arrow was an Arabic one.
It was the one that had two feathers, and I split it between the windpipes. It had penetrated his throat, so I extracted it gently until it came out, and the Christian recovered and was cured. I also extracted another arrow from a man who was struck in his belly, and we estimated that he would die from it.
He remained for a period of thirty days or so, and nothing changed in his condition. I split the arrow and maneuvered to extract it, and he recovered without anything harmful happening to him. I saw a man struck by an arrow on his back, and the wound healed over it.
After seven years, the arrow emerged from the base of his thigh. I saw a woman who was struck in her belly by an arrow; the wound healed, but the arrow remained embedded, and nothing changed in her condition, nor did she find it harmful to any of her natural actions.
I saw another man who was hit by an arrow in his face, and the wound united, and he did not find much pain from it. Such cases are many. I removed an arrow from a commander of the Sultan who had been struck in the middle of his nose, which had slightly tilted to the right side.
The arrow was completely gone. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck, and found the arrow wound very narrow. I probed it with a thin probe and did not feel any obstruction, but he felt a depression and pain on his right side under his ear.
I hoped that this depression was caused by the arrow's tip. I dressed the wound with a dressing that had strong suction and expectorant properties, hoping that it would swell and show an arrow mark. I probed further, but nothing happened in the wound that indicated the arrow had reached the area.
I continued to dress the wound with the dressing for many days, and nothing happened. I applied a cautery within the wound, and the patient remained hopeful but unable to remove the arrow. Eventually, he felt the arrow inside his nose one day, so he informed me. I then applied strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until the wound opened and I felt the thin arrow tip sticking to the wood. I then increased the application of the strong, corrosive medicine to further open the wound. The arrow tip became visible, and it took me about four months to remove it.
Then, when the wound expanded and I was able to insert forceps into it, I pulled and moved the arrow. It did not respond by coming out. I continued to gently treat it and devise methods using various instruments until one day I was able to grasp it with tight forceps, as illustrated in the later part of this chapter.
And the doctors had judged that his nasal cartilage would not heal. He healed it, and the wound mended, and the patient recovered completely, nothing troubling him at all. I will inform you of the method for extracting some arrows so that you may use this as a measure and example for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and their detail cannot be grasped by description nor encompassed by a book. Only the skilled craftsman measures the little by the much, infers what is absent from what is present, and deduces new works and new machines in times of strange emergencies.
If you descend from this craft, I say that arrows are extracted from the parts of the body in which they became embedded in two ways: either by pulling from the place where it entered or by extracting from the opposite side, the one that exited from where it entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshly place, in which case it should be pulled out and extracted. If it does not come out by itself from the position where it entered, you should leave it for several days until the flesh around it decays, making it easier to pull and extract. Similarly, if it becomes embedded in bone.
And if it does not come out by itself, leave it for several days and then pull and move it again each day; it will eventually come out. If it still does not come out after some days, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone from every direction with a fine needle to widen the passage for the arrow, then pull and extract it.
If the arrowhead is embedded in the skull and has penetrated one of the brain cavities, and some of the symptoms mentioned to you have appeared from below, then refrain from pulling out the arrow and leave it until its condition becomes clear after a few days.
If it is necessary to draw out and remove the arrow, if the arrowhead is firmly embedded and does not respond to drawing, use probes around the arrow as described to you, then treat the area until it heals. If the arrowhead has withdrawn to a position in the body, And if it has lost sensation, probe it with a探针，如果感觉到箭头的存在，则使用适当的器械将其拔出。如果由于伤口狭小、箭头深入且位置较深，并无骨头、神经或血管，无法使用上述方法。
And if it has lost sensation, probe it with a probe until you enlarge the wound and can reach the arrow. If it has two ends, grasp them and cleanse the pus from all sides of each end with every means possible. If you cannot completely remove the pus by grasping the ends, break them and probe until you can extract it.
And if you attempt to remove the arrow in any position, use your forceps with all possible angles until you extract it. Be as gentle as possible to avoid breaking the arrow, which would make it difficult to draw out and remove. If you are unable to do so within your means, wait until you can.
Leave it for several days until that flesh around it putrefies, then attack him; if you encounter bleeding, use the treatment we mentioned in its chapter, and spare your effort from cutting vein or nerve or tendon and use巧代手法。
You should extract the arrow from every direction you can with kindness and patience as you described, and you should pull the arrow so that the patient returns to the condition he was in before the arrow struck him; this is more appropriate.
If you cannot do that, use whatever means are available. As for the arrow that comes out on the opposite side, either something protruded from it externally, or you will find the tip of the arrow by touch near the surface of the skin and recognize its direction.
Make an incision in him, and the size of the incision should be commensurate with what the tweezers can accommodate. Then pull it; this will make it easier for it to come out. If it gets stuck in a bone, grasp your hand around the roundness until the arrow affects the bone and widens for itself, then pull it.
Otherwise, leave it for several days until he attacks and comes to you again. If the arrow has broken inside him, push it out with your hand. If the shaft has fallen and you wish to use a probe, insert the hollow instrument into the wound, enter its cavity, then push the arrow out with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with an instrument that can enter that cavity; the arrow will be easier to extract this way. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cauterize the flesh in which all the poison has settled, if you can do so, then treat it accordingly.
If the arrow is found in the chest or in the abdomen or in the bladder or in the groin and it is close to what can be probed with a probe, and you are able to cut through it, then cut through it while being careful not to cut an artery or nerve, and extract it.
Then suture the wound if it needs suturing, and treat it until he is cured. Image of the hooks used to extract arrows:
Its ends are similar to bird beaks, made like forceps so that when they grasp the arrow or anything else, they do not leave it. They can be made in various sizes, large, small, and medium, all according to the size of the arrow and its width, as well as the breadth and narrowness of the wound.

Image of the silenced trumpet:
This is a silenced tip like a mute, to facilitate its entry into the evacuated arrow and push it out.
Fifth Ninety-Chapter
In Treating Arteries.
Arteries that are commonly treated in the body number thirty. Sixteen of these are in the head: the two pulsating arteries behind the ears, known as the Hassasins, and the two arteries in the cheeks, which are prominent, and the two arteries in the area of the eyes, known as the Nozzardin.
And the artery that runs in the middle of the forehead, the artery that is at the tip of the nose, the two arteries in the cheeks, and the two arteries in the upper lip, and the two arteries in the lower lip. These are known as the Jihark. And the two arteries beneath the tongue.
As for the veins that divide in the arm and hand, they are five veins. One is the cephalic, which is from the beastly side, and the common people call it the head vein. The second is the median, which is composed of a branch of basilic and a branch from the cephalic, and the common people call it the body vein.
And the basilic, which is located on the human side and is also called the ulnar vein. The common people call it the abdominal vein. The radial vein, which is situated on the tendon and is the one that protrudes clearly above the thumb. The asilam.
And this is the vein between the middle finger and the ring finger, which has two branches. In the leg, there are three veins. One is below the patella from the beastly side, the second is the saphenous, located on the human side at the heel, and the third is the great saphenous vein, located on the beastly side at the heel.
And in the other leg, there are three veins similar to them. As for the two veins behind the ears, their division is beneficial for chronic and scrofulous diseases, and head wounds with persistent severe sores. The method of dividing them as I describe.

Or in a similar manner, drain the amount of blood you desire. Then tie them with ligatures and leave them until they heal. The Sixty-Ninth Chapter.
On Cupping and Its Usage.
The cups may be made of horns, wood, brass, or glass. Cupping can be performed in two ways: one is cupping with a jar and extracting blood, and the other is cupping without a jar.
And this cupping without a jar can be performed in two ways.
Cupping of the submaxillary regions, which are the sides of the neck, and cupping of the chin, which is under the lower jaw. Cupping of the shoulders, cupping of the sternum, which is at the base of the throat, and cupping of the elbows, which are in the middle of the upper arms, and cupping of the legs, and cupping of the knees.
Cupping draws blood from the fine vessels scattered in the flesh. Therefore, it does not weaken the strength as much as cupping for a fasad. Cupping should not be used with fire or without fire in any condition caused by excess until the body expels all of it.
If the need arose for cupping in illness or by custom, we used it at any time in the beginning of the month and at the end of it and in the middle of it and at whatever time it was.
And this is because some people, when there is an excess of blood in them to the extent that it needs to be removed by cupping, find a heaviness and headache in their head. Some find swelling and redness in their face, head, and neck. Some find itching on their face and armpits, as well as dimness and soreness in their eyes. Some scratch the place where they were cupped.
And some find an increased tendency to laugh. Some find the taste of blood in their mouth and vomit blood. Some sleep more than usual. Some see during their dreams blood, swelling, soreness, and wounds, and things like that. Whenever we saw any of these.
And especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we ordered cupping after two or three hours had passed from noon. As for the benefit of cupping on the nasa'ah point, it benefits from heaviness in the head and a fluid that flows to the eyes.
But it should be done after the body has fully expelled its contents. This cupping can be a substitute for bleeding the qiffal. It is warned that it should not be used by those with cold brains or those who have a fever, as it can cause great harm to them.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly and those with cold head ailments, as well as those who have frequently used it, as it has caused them to forget.
Therefore, it should be ordered that the cupper lower his hand with the cup slightly downwards to avoid causing forgetfulness. As for cupping the shoulder, it is a substitute for bleeding the acromion and basilicus veins.
Therefore, it is effective for asthma, shortness of breath, collapse of the respiratory apparatus, coughing, and congestion. It is necessary to slightly raise cupping on the scapula, for if it is directed downwards, it produces weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping on the cheeks, it is effective for acute headaches in the head, conjunctivitis, malaria, suffocation, and pain in the roots of the teeth. It is a substitute for basilic vein bloodletting. It is necessary to instruct the cupper not to deepen his hand with the suction to avoid cutting a vessel and causing bleeding.
As for cupping under the cheekbones, it is effective for abscesses in the mouth and gum decay and similar diseases of the mouth. It can serve as a substitute for bloodletting of the jaws located in the lips. As for cupping on the shoulders, it is effective for suffocation that results from congestion and heat.
As for cupping on the subclavian region, it is effective for what is effective in bloodletting of the three major veins: the basilic, internal jugular, and external jugular, because it draws blood from all those thin veins within the flesh and draws those thin veins from thicker veins until the suction reaches the three thick veins.
It is necessary to instruct the cupper not to apply the suction too deeply, for the area is bare of flesh and has nerves and vessels underneath. As for single cupping on the scapula, it is effective for hemorrhoids and wounds in the lower region.
It is necessary to instruct the cupper to use a large cup and one made of brass, for the area requires strong suction, and sometimes a glass cup may break and apply too much pressure. As for cups used on the legs, they reduce congestion noticeably.
Because it draws blood from the entire body and benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, wombs, and bladders, and induces menstruation and benefits from pimples and bruises, and serves the place of cupping the spines and ribs except that it exhausts the body greatly and causes dizziness in most people, and the decanters of the ribs' cups have their benefit close to the benefit of cupping the legs.
The method of placing the decanter is to first place the cup empty and suck moderately. Do not prolong the placement of the decanter but put it on quickly and remove it quickly to receive the mixtures evenly. You should repeat this and continue until you see the area turn red and open up.
The blood turns red. Then you should prick and suck gradually. Afterwards, observe the condition of the bodies. If someone has soft flesh with open pores, it is advisable to prick him once only, lest the area become inflamed.
You should direct the cupper to widen the incision, deepen it slightly, and perform the sucking gently with a delicate movement. If the blood is thick, he should prick twice; in the first time, to open a path for the clear and watery part of the blood, and in the second time, to draw out the thick blood.
If the blood is extremely thick, he should prick again a third time to reach the desired level. In summary, if we wish to draw out only a little blood, one incision will suffice; if we wish to draw out much blood, we should make a larger incision. If we determine that the blood is thick, we should follow the appropriate procedure accordingly.
We must stipulate a deep condition and the moderate limit in the condition is only the depth of the skin. What should not be used from ointments when placing the cups and from water, and what the cupped should beware of, as for those whose skin is thick, hard, and tight with narrow pores.
You should apply ointments softening and opening to the areas where the cups will be placed, unless it is in the summer time, then you may use ointment of safflower or balsam or sweet almond ointment or pumpkin seed ointment.
And in the winter, you may use ointment of narcissus or violets or marigold or lilies and similar. If the phlegm is thick and cold, then use ointment of marjoram or nammam or bane or shibth and similar.
If the cupped person has wide pores and loose flesh, it should be avoided to use ointments. These should be washed with rose water or cold water or grape hyacinth water or pumpkin water or dianthus water and similar. As for those whose blood is much wet.
He should wash his cups with vinegar or mint water or similar. As for those whose phlegm is thick, he should wash his cups with old wine or marjoram water or boiled shibth or marigold and similar. It is advisable to avoid cupping in a bathhouse or shortly after a bath.
But it should be used after leaving the bathhouse for an hour or two. It should not be allowed for anyone to sleep after cupping, and proper arrangements should be made by both the cupped person and the therapist before and after cupping.
You should first examine whether the leecher or bloodletter is sanguineous, and if his blood is generally sharp and inflammatory; in which case, he should take the remedies like pomegranate and colocynth with vinegar, lettuce and cardamom and similar ones. His food should be lentils and sheep's meat as well as barley and similar items.
And if his temperament is cold, he should be given honey drink or colocynth decoction or essence. He should take moderate perfumed wine that lies between the old and the new. He should be ordered to eat less, with food consisting of lentils, fava beans, pigeons, and domestic fowl as barley. The drink should be more than the food on the day of bloodletting and blood extraction.
And it should be given at some times to some people of the terryq al-Farouq or musk medicine or sibilatha before bloodletting and before blood extraction or after them, to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood. It should not be administered to those who have recently had bloodletting.
As for the bloodletting without a condition, it is the bloodletting placed on the liver and spleen and the breasts, and on the abdomen and groin, and the kidney area and the sides of the hips, because these organs cannot tolerate a condition on them. Rather, it is intended either to draw the blood from one organ to another.
As we place the leech on the breasts in a case of hemoptysis, or we use it to release wind with cold vapor from bloodletting in an organ. For example, we place the leech on the abdomen and groin, as this agitates the organ, warms it, and relieves pain through the dispersion of that cold vapor.
And it is placed on the kidney if a clot or stone is present, for by the strength of its suction, perhaps it may open the clot or dislodge the stone from its place. Similarly, it acts when placed on the liver and spleen during an attack that affects them; these probes can be used empty only by suction.
And it may be used with fire and may be filled with lukewarm water for cases of scalding. This is done by filling the probe with hot water alone or with water that has been boiled with some herbs suitable for such conditions.
And it is placed filled with water at the affected area, held and removed, and repeated as needed. This is a depiction of the probe used with fire.
Its mouth has the capacity of two open fingers as depicted, and its depth is about half a span. On one side, there is a small hole of about the size that an needle can enter. It is made from Chinese copper or yellow brass with thick edges.
Smooth and level, coated to prevent it from injuring the organ when placed on it. In its middle, there is a slit made of copper or iron through which the candle can be inserted when used with fire. This probe can be made larger than described or smaller according to the illness.
And its method of use is that the probes for boys and eunuchs are different from those for men. As for placing this probe with fire on the organ, it is done by lighting a wick from tightly woven cotton or a small candle, and placing it in the middle of the hardened member located in the center of the probe.
Let the fire rise upwards towards the bottom of the suction cup so as not to burn the patient's body, then place it on the part and have the finger on the hole we mentioned until we grasp the suction cup; then we remove the finger.
The vapor came out through that hole and the suction cup loosened on the site. Then the wick is placed as described and returned if needed. As for the suction cup used for water blisters, it does not have a stiff rod nor a hole.
It is used by filling it with water and placing it only on the part. This is its image:.
This suction cup should be large to hold much water; the larger it is, the better.
Seventh and Ninety-Second Section.
On Treating Boils.
Boils are used most often in parts of the body where it is impossible to place the suction cups either because they are too small, such as the lips and palate, or because the part is bare of flesh, such as the fingers and nose.
The method of using it is to select a boil that is in fresh, pure water free from impurities. Then leave it in fresh water for a day and night until it becomes hungry and there is nothing left in its cavity. Then the patient should first induce vomiting by emesis or by cupping, and then the healthy part should be wiped.
Until it becomes red, then place the suction cup on it. If it fills up and falls off, that is better for its benefit. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar, then with much water, and rub and squeeze it. If the bleeding continues after the boil falls off, and this is a hemorrhage, soak a cloth in cold water and apply it.
And place it from above until the effusion stops. If the blood flows in excess, sprinkle on it a thick powder or ointment or something similar from clamps until the blood stops or place on the place half-beanshell peeled.
And leave it until the beanshell adheres to the place; then the blood will stop. It is necessary that if reapplication is needed, do not reapply that dressing if possible with another, and if dressing cannot adhere, then wipe the place with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the place until it extracts some blood, then place it. If it feels anything of blood, it will adhere to the spot. If you wish to remove it, sprinkle something of acrid powder or salt or ashes on it; it will fall off from the spot.
The Third Chapter.
In the Jawb (abscess).
This chapter also belongs to the artifice needed in the craft of medicine, which is the treatment of fractures and dislocations in bones.
Know, O my sons, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people. Those who have never studied the works of the ancients in it or read a single word from them.
And for this reason, this art has become non-existent in our land. Indeed, I have never come across anyone who was satisfactory in it at all. And I have derived from it only what I derived through extensive reading of the works of the ancients and my earnest desire to understand them, until I extracted the knowledge of that from them. Then I adhered to experimentation and practice throughout my life.
And I have outlined to you from this in its entirety what my science encompasses. And I have tried it through my experience after bringing it to you, refining it from the trappings of prolixity and condensing it to the utmost brevity, elucidating it to the greatest extent of clarity, and illustrating therein many images of the machines used in it.
And it is for the increase in clarity, as I did in the preceding two chapters. And there is no strength except in Allah, the Most High, the Greatest.
The First Chapter.
It is advisable to summarize and condense matters concerning the breaking of bones, and to present them before we begin to mention each broken and dislocated part individually. It should be necessary to include in the beginning of this chapter some statements and sections. It will first compel you and anyone else who is eager to learn this noble art to understand them and grasp their reality.
I say that whenever a person suffers a break, dislocation, inflammation, or fall, one should first hasten to reduce it, evacuate it, or treat both conditions. If there is no obstacle preventing this, such as weakness of strength or if the incident occurred to a child, an old man who is feeble-minded, or if the time is extremely hot or cold.
Then his diet should be limited to cool pulses and bird meat and broth. He should avoid heavy foods and overeating, and any food that fills the veins with blood. Until swelling is secured and you do not expect a discharge to the area.
When then the patient returns to his first regimen which was customary for him, if the broken bone is in the process of setting it should be nourished with foods that are rich, thick, and strong, containing gelatine like lentils, rice, heads, camels' hump, and calves' heads. And eggs, tender fish, thick drinks, and the like, for by this regimen the healing of the fracture is faster and better. Know that broken bones in strong men and old men cannot ever connect and fuse back to their original state.
Due to the dryness of their bones and their hardness, what was extremely flexible in the bones can connect and fuse like the bones of young children. However, nature grows on all sides of the broken bone something similar to glue, making it hard, adhering, and drawing it together until it binds with itself.
It binds with itself until it attains the utmost strength and firmness as it was originally, so that nothing in his actions hinders him.
For this reason, the patient's diet should consist of foods that are strong, sticky, and hard, as we have said. Know that fractures can differ according to the type of bone involved, for a fracture of the thigh is different from a fracture of the skull, and a fracture of the chest is different from a fracture of the spine.
Similarly, all other organs differ from one another. We will describe each type of fracture in detail in its own section, differentiated from others. Moreover, the fracture of a bone itself can also vary in different ways.
Because it may be a fracture due to a contusion without fragments occurring, and it may be a fracture along the bone with fragments and displaced pieces, whether completely or partially, and it may be a fracture with wound and breach in the skin, and it may be a slight crack.
And each type has a special method for setting it, as will be detailed in its place. As for the signs of bone fracture, they are its bending, displacement, and palpability, as well as its tremor when you touch it with your hand.
And if there is no obvious bending, tremor, or sensitivity in the area, and the bone does not show much disturbance upon palpation, and the patient experiences little pain, then there is no fracture. It could be a sprain or a slight fracture or a minor crack, so it should not be moved at all by stretching or probing.
But apply to it the medications that will be mentioned later as appropriate for the area. Then, tie it gently. Know that if the bone separates and pierces through two points without any fragments occurring, except that each part has separated from its counterpart.
But you should not hastily attempt to correct and align it before it develops a hot swelling. If it does develop a hot swelling, leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides. Then, adjust and align it gently using any means at your disposal that are gentle and skillful.
Know that setting and aligning it is easier when there are no fragments in the bone. Then, tie up as will be mentioned. If the bone has fragments, it is necessary to extend the broken limb from both sides whether by hand or foot.
Either by hand if the member is small, or with threads, or with thread and hand. Let the position of your member be on a level place in its natural shape until if the extended part of the broken bone reaches there; then return those excesses to their places by every means you can manage of巧思和温柔。
Strive to avoid causing further injury with your actions and do not bring pain or swelling. Strive to join one bone to its fellow in the best possible alignment. At that time, you should feel and examine them with your hand. If you see anything out of place, correct it and straighten it as well as you can.
Be careful to avoid excessive stretching and strong probing, as many of the ignorant often do. They frequently cause severe swelling or prolonged pain in the member through their actions, as you have seen this happen repeatedly.
Then, after the correction and perfection of the tension, keep that member at rest and allow it to heal. Warn the patient not to move it during both wakefulness and sleep, and when turning or disturbing himself, and during defecation and all his movements as much as he can. He should take care to position the member in a way that ensures safety from pain.
And this is because if the patient feels pain or discomfort in the position he has placed the member, he should shift it to another position where he does not feel pain. He should also ensure that this new position is level and straight.
Lest there be an awkwardness in the member if it breaks and heals straight. As for the way of tensioning the broken member, it is as I have described to you. Know that broken members differ in their size, shape, and form; some being small like the arm, finger, and thigh, and similar parts.
It is necessary that the bandages for injuries be soft and gentle, and those of the thigh, back, and chest should be broad and solid, as the wide ligament must bind the large member equally from all sides without any imbalance.
Then you begin to apply the appropriate paint after completing the smoothing. In applying gentle pressure to the break, then you begin to wrap the ligament around the break itself three or four times depending on what the member requires, and you gently tighten the ligament with your hand. Then you bring it to the upper part of the break position.
With less tension than you use for the broken area, then you wrap a bit away from the break and gradually relax the tension until you get a suitable position. Then you take another bandage and wrap it also around the broken area.
Wrap several times, then bring the wrap to the lower part of the break. Your action in tightening and relaxing the wrap should follow what we have mentioned for the first upper wrap. Then place between the bandages some soft pressure or padding to straighten out the break.
If there is a curvature, then put something to correct it. If not, do not place anything. Then wrap it with another bandage and apply the tight splints from your hand if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the member.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, apply what alleviates that inflammation and relieves the swelling. Leave it for a few days, then tighten the splints when doing so. The splints should be made of carved and prepared halves of broad bamboo rods, or they can be made of willow wood.
which is made from pine or palm leaves or rush or reed and whatever is available of such materials, let the size of each bandage be like this shape and form.
Except that the bandage which is placed on the fracture itself should be wider and slightly thicker than the other bandages. As for the length of the bandage, it should be made according to what is suitable for the broken limb in terms of its size, whether large or small. The shape of the bandage:
Then tie the bandages with another wrap according to your initial tightness. Afterwards, tie it from above with tight threads as we have mentioned, which is that your tightening should be more at the site of the fracture. The tighter the binding should be the closer it is to the fracture.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and softness. They should be made of wet cotton thread, especially, because if the threads are too thick like what you have seen from the actions of the ignorant, they make their threads from rolled cotton fabric, which is a great mistake.
Because it causes the binding to be too tight,偏离中庸；而太薄的线也不合适，因为你无法通过它达到所需的紧度。相邻的绷带之间不应少于一个手指的宽度。
If the patient is affected by the edges of the bandages after tight binding in the correct places, place underneath them something soft like cotton or fluffy wool so that nothing from it affects him. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and there is a tear in the skin, we will discuss it separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be set with splints from the first day. For when the limb is large, it should not be placed on splints except after five days or seven or more, according to your safety from the occurrence of the hot swelling.
This is the characteristic of the bandages that were used to immobilize the initial injuries, such as fractures, jaws, and thighs. It is a general brief bandage that sets the fracture and suits most conditions, especially children and women, because it does not predominantly have heat or cold.
Take the dust from the grain mill, which is the fine flour that sticks to the walls of the mill when the grinding is done. Knead it as it is without sieving it with egg white, making the paste neither too thick nor too thin. Then apply it.
The characteristic of another bandage that sets fractures, dislocations, and thighs: Take from the maash, ladan, aqiqya, rasn, mghath, and saqq, each eleven dirhams. From the murra and sabur, five dirhams each. From athl, twenty dirhams. And from Armenian or Roman clay, twenty dirhams. Grind all the ingredients and mix them with water of athl or with egg white if the patient's condition is feverish. Then use this bandage, for it is well-compounded, sets broken bones quickly, and suits most people due to its moderation.
The characteristic of another bandage that is also useful for fractures and thighs: Take mghath, maash, and white khattami from each eleven dirhams. Take murra and sabur from each five dirhams. Take aqiqya six dirhams and Armenian clay twenty dirhams. Grind all the ingredients finely, sieve them, and mix with water or egg white. Then use it.
Description of a dressing for dislocated joints and bones and to alleviate pain associated with them, fractures, and bone displacement. Take the purified wool and soak it in vinegar and cooked oil, then place it on the area. This dressing does not have the power of reduction.
But it is superior in alleviating the hot swelling and repelling pain specifically. Description of another dressing to reduce a fracture, take the bark of the deaf vine and the wild licorice leaf, crush them both together, and apply with a wet cloth. Description of another brief dressing used when a fracture dislocation occurs.
To resolve the remaining swelling, take one part of saffron, one part of chamomile, and one part of madder. Crush all together and mix with paint if the limb is not swollen; if it is swollen, mix it with fenugreek water or water. Use it accordingly.
Description of another stronger dressing for resolving hard swelling when a fracture dislocates, take one part each of saffron, cottonseed, fennel, crown flower, marigold, madder, and chamomile.
Crush all together and mix with water of conflict, or fresh water, or paint, according to the heat of the limb and the quieting of its fever. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be applied to the broken limb during reduction.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil, nothing else. It should be moderate between hardness and softness. As for Galen, he believed that things with dryness should be applied to the broken limb during reduction, such as bitter substances, patience, frankincense, and similar materials as mentioned.

Then Place Thereon Softened Cotton Soaked in Vinegar and Oil or Rose Anointing Oil, and Bind It Thereewith for One Night Until You Fear No Hot Swelling And Alleviates the Swelling and Alleviates the Pain. Then Return It to Gentle Tying and a Light Plaster But Do Not Bind It As Tight As Initially.
And Attend to It Until It Heals. If You See That the Swelling, Redness, Pain, and Swelling Have Gone Completely and You Need a Plaster and Tight Binding Restore It as You Did Initially, For If Nothing We Mentioned Occurs in the Part.
Do Not Dissolve It Except After Three or Four or Five or Seven Days, and It May Be Left for Twenty Days. All of This Depends on How the Part Appears to You, as We Have Said, Until the Fracture Remains and the Flesh近处的伤口接近愈合。
Increase the tension more than your first tension altogether. Also increase the severity of the food for the sick person more than what was mentioned before, for if you see that the fracture site has dried up and become softer than it should, know that the food is not reaching it.
Dilute it with fresh water every time you prepare it and let this be done every three days. Lightly decrease the tension; by this action, the food will reach the organ and heal quickly. As for what the ignorant ones do, who force the break again on the organ, it is incorrect.
If it does not heal properly at first and heals with a deformity instead, this is an error in their action and a great deceit. If it were correct, they would have mentioned it first in their books and acted upon it. And I have never seen any of them leave any trace of doing so at all. The correct action is not to perform it.
Chapter Two.
On the Fracture of the Head.
The types of fractures in the head are many, and their forms vary. The causes are diverse. Some fractures occur due to a sword strike and may involve the entire bone, extending down to the table beneath the bone. as is done in splitting wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a "qadumiyya." It may involve the cutting of the sword through part of the bone and only abrading its surface without penetrating to the end. This type of fracture is also called a "qaul" or complete cut. The wound from these two fractures may be either large or small.
And some fractures are brittle or crushed and their cause is a blow with a stone or falling on a stone or the like. This fracture may be penetrating and nearly reaches the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be in the face of the bone.
And the wound of these fractures may also be either large or small. Some fractures are hidden in the bone in the soft hair and are a slight depression.
And therefore, this type of fracture is called "sha'ri" (hair). Such a fracture may occur due to falling or striking with a stone and the like, where the bone page enters inward, causing depression in the area, similar to how brass would凹陷 if struck and part of it enters inward.
And this is more common in the wet bones of the head, such as the skulls of boys. Such fractures may have detached or undetached fragments, and we will mention the treatment of all these types in their respective places. We will recognize all these types of fractures by examining them.
And examine it with probes, and remove the rotten flesh from them. As for the hair fracture, it is recognized by examining the bone, wiping it, and applying ink to it; the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of the fracture, first consider the symptoms of the patient.
If you see in its symptoms clear indications of fear, such as bitter vomiting, stretching, loss of consciousness, fainting, high fever, dim and red eyes, and other similar symptoms, do not treat the patient or approach him; for death is certain to occur in most cases when accompanied by these symptoms.
And if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his safety, then begin his treatment. This is when the wounded one comes to you in the first instance of injury and this was during days of winter.
It is advisable to strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case, and if it is during the days of summer, it is advisable to hasten the removal of the bone before the seventh day, lest what is below the bone from the membrane become spoiled; exposing those symptoms which we have described.
If the fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is also comminution and softening, it is advisable to amputate the comminuted and softened part as I have described to you, which is to shave the head of the wounded patient.
And expose the bone in any way that is feasible for you and according to the shape of the wound and what is visible on the patient. If upon exposing the bone, you observe bleeding or a hot swelling, treat it as necessary.
And do this by stuffing the area with a soaked cloth in a mixture of wine and oil, and cleanse the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding is controlled. Then proceed with drilling the bone and removing it, which can be done in one of two ways.
As for the one way, it is to cut the bone with a gentle incision using a narrow blade. This is its form:.
Then use another incision after this one, slightly broader. This is its form:.
Then use another incision, also broader than the second. It is necessary for you to have a variety of blades, some broader and some shorter than others, with their edges in the utmost sharpness. They should be made of Indian steel or good steel.
Use kindness in striking the severed part lest the head be dislodged and hurt. If the bone is strong and hard, it should be drilled around before using the cutting forceps they named non-imbedding forceps. They should be called non-imbedding forceps.
Because it does not exceed the limit of the bone of the forehead to what is behind it, for the forceps has a round handle below its sharp head resembling a ring or small circle that prevents it from imbedding and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
You should take many of these forceps, each suitable for the thickness of that bone. Each forcep should be prepared to fit the size of the forehead, with its sharp end length and shortness according to the thickness of that forehead. This is a picture of three types. The forceps are large, medium, and small.
As for the method of drilling around the broken bone, you should place the forceps on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has given way. Then move the forceps to another position and make sure the distance between each drill is about the thickness of the marrow or something similar.
Then cut with the cutting forceps between each two drill holes and do this with as much kindness as you can, as we said, until you remove the bone either by hand or with some other instrument from among the tools you have prepared for this purpose.
For example, use the gentle forceps and clamps. You should be very careful that the forceps or cutter do not touch the dura mater. If you have reinforced the bone and it is free from the dura mater, and you have removed it, it should be stripped clean.
And to smooth the remaining roughness in the bone, use another instrument resembling the saw, except that it should be thinner and softer than other saws. If there is any small bones or碎片 remaining, take them with care using the instruments at your disposal.
Then treat the wound with sutures and ointments that will be mentioned. As for the other side of the work, it is a matter easy to determine and far from danger, about which Galen spoke and praised greatly. This is his saying. You should first begin by exposing the part of the bone in the area where the fracture is most severe and prominent. Once you have exposed that part, place the tip of this convex saw beneath it; this is its shape:.
The convex part of the saw should be smooth and not cut through anything, while the sharp part on its sides should extend in length as you see. This allows the convex part to rest against the table, and the sharp edge of the saw to contact the bone. Then strike the saw from one side.
With a small mallet until you cut through the entire bone with care, as it turns. You are safe from the membrane, and nothing terrifying will happen, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and胆怯的人，确实是这样，即使他是在睡觉。
If there is something adhering to the bone from the membrane in some parts of the bone, scrape it off with the tip of the convex saw itself, and remove it carefully. It can be removed without any pain or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone did not penetrate to the membrane, and what broke off from the bone was its end, and there remained roughness and fine碎片 in it, you should remove that roughness and extract those fragments with delicate probes that have been prepared in various sizes so that you can use the most suitable and appropriate one for each place as the work dictates.
The same bone and its shape, it is incumbent upon you to use in your inventory first the coarser instruments for the bone, then use after them finer ones. You must continue to do this with loyalty until you reach the use of its finest and most delicate ones.
As for other fine hairline fractures and gentle breaks, it is incumbent upon you to use for each one a treatment according to what leads to its correction, and this is something that does not escape him who has the slightest skill in this craft and has stopped by what we have written and explained regarding major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after extracting the bone, it is incumbent upon you to take a cotton cloth of the size of the wound and dip it in rose oil ointment. Place it over the opening of the wound, then take another cloth folded or triangular. Dip it in a mixture of juice and rose oil ointment, place it over the first cloth, and do this with the lightest material you can manage so as not to burden the dressing. Then use an宽腰带覆盖在上面，不要勒得太紧，只需足够紧以固定伤口。
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of the subsidence of the hot swelling, then remove it and use instead the medicines that have a drying effect such as poppy roots, marshmallow powder, ground colocynth, and safflower, and the like. You should prepare these medicines as powders.
And apply it to the wound as a powder. In general, use a medicine that is purgative and not irritating. Strive to keep your treatment of the wound clean, avoiding any irritation from ointment, neither being too dirty nor too clean, and never allow pus to accumulate in it.
Because the pus, if it meets with the meningeal membrane, corrupts and putrefies it. Great debility results from this for the patient, and it may appear in the cerebral spinal fluid when the bone is revealed, especially if treatment is neglected.
Blackness on its surface, if observed and the patient exhibits the symptoms we have mentioned, know that it is hopeless without doubt. And if the blackness occurred due to a medicine placed thereon and was caused by the strength of that medicine, know that it does not signify hopelessness.
You should take a part of honey and three parts of rose fat, mix them well, apply them to a cloth, then place it on the cerebral spinal fluid. Then treat him with various treatments that are appropriate until he recovers.
The Third Chapter.
To reduce the nose if it is broken.
Know that the nose breaks only if its upper part, or one of them, fractures. This is because they are bones; the lower part is cartilaginous and does not break, but it may become swollen, crooked, or puffy.
If one of its halves fractures, you should insert a small finger into the nostril and straighten the fracture from inside with your middle finger and press it from outside to restore the nose to its natural shape. Do this gently and take care that you do not cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger did not reach it, you should straighten it with the tip of a splint that has a slight thickness. If the fracture is on both sides, do likewise. It should be reduced as soon as possible on the first day after the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot tumor is quiescent, then insert a cotton wick into the nasal aperture, one if the break was on one side or two if the break was on both sides.
Let the wick be thick enough to fill the nasal aperture. Some of the early restorers have mentioned that the wick should be soaked in oil and replaced each day. I do not see this as necessary; rather, it should be soaked in the white of an egg mixed with flour dust.
Then leave the wick until the bone has set and the cartilage is hardened. You may insert into the nose, in place of the wick, tubes made of goose feathers, wrapped with a soft linen to make it easier to hold due to the fracture of the nose, so that the patient does not find it difficult to breathe.
This is not essential; if you wish to make the wicks, or the tubes, you may do so. If a hot tumor arises in the nose during your work, dress the nose with cerate, or with cotton soaked in vinegar and oil or rose fat, or something from the ointment of diachylon.
If a hot tumor does not arise, it should be dressed externally with fine flour and pounded cornflower that has been mixed with the white of an egg. Then place a soft pad on top and do not bind the nose at all, for if the bones of the nose break in small fractures.
Or if it breaks, it should be incised and removed with the instrument suitable for this. Then suture the incision and treat it with a paste or ointment that will heal and seal the wound. If there is a wound inside the nose, it should be treated with a wick, and you should use copper tubes. Until he recovers.
The Fourth Chapter.
To set the lower beard roots that have broken.
If the lower beard roots have broken, and there is no wound, look to see if the break is only from the outside and does not fracture in two and turn inward. Its identification becomes easier.
If the break is in the right side, introduce the third finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth. Similarly, if the break is in the left side, introduce the third finger of the right hand and gently raise the bulge of the break from inside to outside.
With the other hand from outside the bone, control it to straighten it. If the jaw fracture has separated into two parts, it should be reduced using traction from both sides until it can be properly aligned. If it occurred in the teeth, they will grow back.
Or it may separate, so tighten what you hoped would remain with a thread of gold or silver or silk. Then place the copper splint on the broken beard roots, put a folded cloth on top, and cover it with a tight bandage or a piece of leather equal in length to the beard. Then tie it from above as you find convenient, arranging it to hold together without loosening. Order the patient to rest and remain calm, and give him soft food. If you think that anything has changed in its appearance at any point, loosen it on the third day and adjust it.
