Fifth Chapter.
In the Reduction of the Clavicle if Broken.
The clavicle is broken more frequently at the front, near the end of the sternum. Its fracture can occur in one of three ways: either it breaks and splits into two pieces without any fragments, which is the easiest to reduce.
Or the fracture may produce fragments, which is harder to reduce. Or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound. If the fracture is without a wound, prepare two attendants: one to hold the arm below the broken clavicle and the other to extend the neck towards the opposite side.
Then smooth the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be and there is no凹陷或扭曲。如果需要更深入，则应在患者腋下放置一个充气球或棉球。
And its bone should be as much as you need. Extend and lift the scapula, and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is set as it should be. If you are not able to draw the end of the scapula outward because it has gone deep, then the patient should lie on his side.
A medium-sized pillow is placed under his shoulder. The pillow is pressed down on his shoulder until the deep scapula rises to the top. Then, correct the fracture and smooth it with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the scapula has broken and is moving, it should be incised and the fragment removed gently. If the fragment is embedded in the bone, you should try to cut it with one of the instruments prepared for this purpose. After the scapula has been stabilized with a clamp, which is made of wood or iron, this is its shape:.
It resembles a spoon with no handle. Its width should be according to the size of the bone, whether large or small. As for its length, it should also be suitable for the work you need to do. Let it have two ends, as you can see; one end is wider and the other is narrower.
If the wound from which you extracted the bone fragment is wide and there is no danger of a hot abscess, suture the wound's lips together. If the wound is minor or you fear a hot abscess, enlarge the wound by incising it and providing drains according to how much the wound was opened.
If a hot tumor is presented, apply the incision on ointment of rose and vinegar or drink and carry on. As for the tension of bone if it was without wound nor cut, then carry on with the bandage made from flour dust with egg white. You place the soft pad on it, then put the ball under his iliac region if needed. Then take a turban that is extremely long, with its width about a cubit or so, wrap the curved bandage around the pad and bandage, then take a splint from a thin board. Its width should be three fingers and the same for its length. Then insert it into a cloth, then place that cloth with the splint in the position on the turban that corresponds to the location of the fracture. Then tie the turban around the fracture as you wind it. Wrap it around his neck and under his healthy iliac region and his患病侧的髂部，然后每面都缠绕几圈，确保你认为拉紧可以牢固固定骨折。这对你来说是显而易见的。
Make sure that the splint does not come off from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient daily. Whenever you find that the bandage has relaxed and see that the splint has come off, correct this by tightening the bandage. Then make sure the patient sleeps on his back, and place a small pillow under his iliac region at night for support.
Lift it with it so that his shoulder rises above his side, and the fracture of the clavicle will rise with the elevation of the iliac region. Alternatively, tie his arm to his neck. Do not loosen the bandage unless there is a occurrence of itching or tumor in the area for twelve days.
Then renew the bandage if you see a reason for it. Respond to the tension and leave it until the clavicle heals and strengthens, and this becomes stronger and more robust over twenty-eight days. And it may be in some people in less than that.
Chapter Six.
To treat a dislocation of the shoulder. The shoulder rarely breaks at the wide part; it usually breaks at its parts. Whether it breaks at the edge or in the middle, this can be identified by touch. Treatment depends on the shape of the fracture.
Adjust it and return it to its natural shape using any means available. Then, apply a mixture of flour dust and egg white, and place a folded cloth pad on top. Finally, apply a wide bandage made from a board.
Make it as thin as the shoulder or a bit wider. If there is any swelling in any part of the shoulder under the bandage, gently treat that swelling with a soft pad until the bandage settles evenly. Then, tighten it from above with a long scarf.
Tighten it firmly and ensure the bandage stays in place, so it does not shift. Check the ligament daily; if the ligament relaxes, tighten it again and adjust the bandage if it has shifted. Let the patient lie on his sound side. The shoulder will heal in about twenty days.
One day or twenty-five days. If this period is completed and the ligament has healed, you are in safety, as these are bones that do not fear fragmentation or displacement. If a shard appears from the bone and causes irritation under the skin, incise it and extract it. Make what was mentioned regarding the treatment of clavicle fracture in soothing the hot swelling if anything of that kind occurs.
Seventh Chapter.
To correct a broken chest.
The chest may break in its middle, and that is rare. As for its edges, they are more likely to break and fracture. Among its symptoms, if the middle breaks, it will incline downwards and cause severe pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing.
Sometimes, it may bleed and the depression in the broken bone will be evident, not hidden from touch. To correct it, the patient should lie on his back with a cushion placed between his shoulders. Then, his elbows should be pressed, and the ribs gathered by the hands from both sides. Care should be taken to adjust them gently as much as possible on every side.
And adjust it until the shape of the bone returns to what is proper. Then, apply a dressing and pressure, and place over it a splint made from a strip of parchment or similar light material, wrapped in a cloth. And gently secure it around the broken bone so that it does not move. Pass the bandage around in a circular motion towards the back several times, tightening it firmly. Always check the bandage regularly, and tighten it whenever it relaxes.
And if necessity demands that it be loosened due to hunger or pain or swelling in the area, loosen it promptly. Remove the dressing and remedy whatever issue arose through appropriate treatment. Then, reinstate the dressing if you see fit. He treated it and subjected it to tension until it healed.
Chapter Eight.
In reducing the ribs if they are fractured, know that fractures of the ribs occur only in the toughest areas following the spine and their ends in front. Only pressure is applied to them because they are costal and the nature of this can be known by touch when examining with fingers.
Reduce it by aligning the fracture with the fingers until the shape is proper, then apply a compress and splint the broken bone if necessary. If the rib fracture is inclined inward, it will cause severe pain to the patient.
And it is prone to effusion like the effusion that occurs in those with pleurisy, because the bone can cause effusion and also lead to difficulty in breathing, coughing, and the expulsion of much blood, which is a difficult condition to treat. The ancients devised many remedies for this.
Some say that the patient should be given foods that produce flatulence to expand the abdomen and allow the fracture to protrude outward. We dislike this because it may confirm the occurrence of a hot tumor if one does not already exist. If a tumor has developed, however, it will worsen and confirm its presence.
Some said that a diaphoretic should be placed on the area, then firmly suctioned. This is more like a test, except that one fears the diaphoretic might draw fluid to that spot due to its weakened state. Others said that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
And the padding becomes situated between the ribs until it fills out, making the bandage level when rolled. Then the sore is treated with a dressing remedy from food and medicine, for if the sore is pressed, it is a severe condition that cannot be endured. And the bone would pierce the diaphragm painfully and we were afraid for the sore, so it should be necessary to incise the place and uncover the broken rib. Then we would place under it the device that preserves the cranial cavity, which has been described, and cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then we gather the edges of the wound if it is large with suturing and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot swelling appears during this, act quickly by applying padding in rose oil and place it on the area, and treat the sore with what soothes the swelling from within as well.
And he should lie on the side that alleviates his sleep until it heals.
Chapter Nine.
To reduce dislocations of the back and neck.
As for the neck bones, if they suffer a fracture and such injuries are rare, while dislocations are more common. Similarly, the vertebrae of the back also—when such an injury occurs to someone, and you want to know whether it will heal or not. Then examine them and if you see that his hands have become relaxed and paralyzed, having no sensation or movement, unable to stretch, bend, or grasp, and if you press on them or needle them, he does not feel it or find any pain in them, know that in most cases it will not heal and is lost.
And if he moves them and feels with the probe and nuxs, know that the marrow of the bone is safe and the disease will be cured by treatment. If a vertebra is hit like that and you want to know whether it will also be cured or not.
And look at his feet; if you see that they have relaxed and what happened to the hands occurred in them, then when he lies on his back, wind and feces come out without intention, and if he lies on his stomach, urine comes out without intention. And if he lies on his back and intends to urinate but is unable to do so, know that he is lost; do not expect to cure him. If nothing like this occurs, the matter is milder, and the treatment for what has happened is to aim at soothing the hot swelling.
By placing on the tender vertebra either rose oil alone or with roasted egg yolks. Apply this three times a day until the hot swelling subsides, then apply one of the strengthening and drying poultices and bind it with a bandage. Order him to rest and remain calm.
And let him sleep only on the side that does not cause him pain until he recovers. If a shard or something similar was broken in the bone and has been removed, it should be necessary to incise his skin and extract that bone. Then gather the lips of the wound if it is large by suturing, then treat with the healing ointments until it heals. If another bone of the os navale breaks, which is the heel bone, it should be necessary to insert the thumb from the left hand into the acetabulum.
and the broken bone is aligned with the other hand according to what can be done and achieved, then it is wrapped with a bandage and a splint if needed, then it is tied up.
If you feel a broken fragment in it, split it open and extract it, and treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals.
The Tenth Chapter.
To treat a fracture of the hip.
Rarely do the hip bones break; if they do, the break may fragment into pieces, lengthwise split, or tilt inward. The patient will experience pain at the site, swelling, and numbness or paralysis in the leg on the side of the fracture.
To set it, pass your hand over it until you determine the shape of the fracture. If the break is only in the fragments, align those fragments as best you can so that they resemble their natural shape.
If the break is lengthwise or has tilted inward, place the patient on their belly so that you can set this fracture. Once aligned, wrap it with a bandage, then place a splint made of wood or leather over it and tie it securely.
Do not fear the transfer of the fracture or the removal of the splint. Align any displacement in the ilium with what fills it, until it takes on a level position. Order the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side. If a hot swelling occurs, address it accordingly.
Cease to extend or treat it until the hot swelling subsides, then apply what alleviates it as previously mentioned. Afterwards, return to setting and securing it properly. If there are fragments or any part has fractured, it should not be removed.
It should be smoothed from the outside as we said and the tension left until it heals.
The Eleventh Chapter.
On the Reduction of Breaks in the Forearm.
The forearm is the area between the elbow and the shoulder. If it breaks, reduce it in one of two ways. One way is to take a curved, smooth, medium-sized rod in this shape:.
And bind it at both ends with straps. Then suspend it from a high place, and the patient sits on a chair. Then place his broken arm on the rod until his elbow is adhering to the middle of the bend in the rod. Then hang something heavy from above.
Or a servant may extend it downward, then the doctor reduces the break with his hands until the fracture is in the proper position. The other way is for the patient to lie on his back and have his hand tied around his neck with a strap.
Then order servants to hold the upper part of the break with one hand and the lower part with the other. Each should stretch towards their respective sides. If you want the stretching to be stronger, bind below the break with a strap and above it with another, and each servant should stretch them towards their respective sides.
If the break is near the upper arm, the strap should be placed below the elbow and the other below the break towards the shoulder. If the break is near the shoulder, the straps should be placed in those positions and also on the shoulder itself.
Then adjust the fracture gently without violence until the fracture aligns properly and heals well. Then tighten it if no hot swelling appears; if a hot swelling does appear, leave tightening until the seventh day, and apply a dressed cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then tighten it. The method of tightening is to place the dressing over the fracture, then wrap a new cloth around the dressing, then join the arm to the same back and place his hand open on his hip.
And hold the bandage and tension on the back and arm, so that the arm substitutes for the splints if nothing prevents you from doing so and if there is no change in the broken bone. If you fear any change in the broken bone, use the splints.
Place a splint directly on the fracture itself that is wider and stronger than the other splints. There should be an interval of one finger's width between each splint. The length of the splints should correspond to the fracture, with an additional three fingers' width on each side.
Then tighten the splints with the tension you mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
It should be that your tension is stronger at the location of the fracture.
The tension should be less the farther from the fracture. If you see that the splint and tension should be placed as we said when setting the bone, do so.
And if you fear the hot swelling, leave the tension and splints until the seventh day as we said. Then examine the bandage every three days to ensure there is no itching or puffiness, and that nutrition can reach the limb due to excessive tension. Correct all of this as we have mentioned.
If you are certain that nothing of the sort will happen, then release the bandage only after many days, and let the injured lie on his back with his hands on his abdomen, and place under the arm a pillow filled with wool, well-padded.
Examine at all times, day or night, lest the shape of the broken limb be altered or the bandage relax; correct all this by your efforts. And let the diet of the injured be as we have said before, that it should first be gentle, until the bone is about to strengthen.
It is necessary to strengthen his diet, for it is customary for the arm and leg to grow strong in forty days. Then you should release the bandage and use bathing and treatments appropriate for this purpose. If the fracture is severe and displaced, this should be addressed accordingly.
Do not remove the bandage from him until fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the Fracture of the Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, and they are called the radius and ulna. The smaller one follows the hand and is below the larger bone. The larger bone is positioned below the smaller one from below. Sometimes, only the largest bone (radius) breaks, or only the smaller bone (ulna) breaks, and sometimes both break together.
When the upper smaller bone (ulna) breaks, its fracture is easy to treat and heals faster. When the lower bone (radius) breaks, its fracture is worse and harder to heal. If both bones break together, it is the worst. If the upper smaller bone (ulna) breaks, then...
The surgeon should make the duration gentle with care until it is aligned. If the large nail is broken, he should make the duration somewhat stronger. If all the nails are broken, he should make the duration very strong indeed.
The shape of the hand should be considered when setting and extending it on a cushion. The thumb should be above all the fingers, and the index finger should be below the rest. The patient should be seated leaning forward.
Let the cushion be at the same height so that the patient does not incur difficulty.
Then, the arm should be extended from below either by hand or with a bandage. Another attendant should extend it from above similarly. Then, the surgeon should align the bone until he restores it to the best possible shape. If there are fragments in the broken bone, they should be removed.
Restore each fragment in its place as much as you can. If a detached fragment is visible and it causes the skin to peel, and there is no hope of setting it, cut into it and extract it according to the method described earlier. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, it has been given a separate section.
If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of setting, apply a compress of cilantro prepared with rose oil and white wax. Make it moderately thick and tightly but gently bind the cloth around it until the swelling subsides.
Remove the cilantro and apply a compress prepared from flour dust and egg white. Then, place the splints; let the splint that goes on top of the break be slightly wider and stronger. Know that the number of splints for the arm is usually six in most cases.
Was the fracture in one canal or both canals together, then make your pull at the site of the fracture stronger and more intense. And whenever you turn the pull upwards or downwards, make the pull a bit lax as previously mentioned at the beginning of this section.
Let the tear that wraps around the fracture be soft and moist, not too hard. The thread with which you pull, using a special catgut, should be medium between being very thin and thick, as we described. Check the organ and ligament after a few days.
If anything needs to be corrected, such as itching that occurs in the organ, it should be treated by washing the organ with warm water until the itching subsides. The organ should then be left untied for a night until it rests, and then pulling should be resumed.
If the pull has become lax and the bone has moved or if there is any such condition, make every effort to correct it. Also, if the food is prevented from reaching the organ due to excessive pulling, you should relax it a bit and leave it for some days until the food begins to reach it again, then pull it.
If nothing has occurred to the patient as mentioned, he should not be allowed to get up until twenty days or so have passed. Then hang the patient's hand around his neck and let his arm be moderate. He should guard against sudden movements and make his sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm will heal in thirty days or in twenty-nine, and it might heal in twenty-eight days. All of this depends on the conditions of the humors and the strength of the person.
The Thirteenth Chapter.
In the Reduction of Fractures of the Wrist and Fingers.
If the wrist and stability of the fingers are involved, fractures are rare. However, displacements occur frequently. If either the wrist or a displacement of the fingers is fractured.
It is necessary that the patient sit in a reclining position with a chair opposite him levelly.
Then place his hand extended on it. Then the bone setter stretches the broken bones, and the physician sets them until they unite properly. At that point, it is necessary to apply the bandage and splint if no hot swelling occurs, then a cast should be applied from above according to the location.
And I have included it in a soft cloth. If the fracture is downwards towards the palm of the hand, make something resembling a ball from the cloth and instruct the patient to grasp it with his broken hand. Then tie it tightly with a long piece of cloth.
And the cast should be made of leather with a soft lining so that the skin can grow along with the bending of the entire wrist. Tie it tightly as needed. If the fracture is outward, the cast should be applied from above and another one underneath the wrist to keep the hand open and straight.
Then, apply the tension as the hand turns, and tie the fingers together with the bandage. If the fracture involves one of the stability ligaments of the fingers, set it properly, then tighten it along with the wrist. If you wish, make a small standing cast to set the fracture.
No movement should occur; if the fracture is in any of the other fingers like the ring finger, middle finger, or thumb, then it should be aligned and fixed with the correct finger next to it, or all of them should be fixed together by ligature, which is better, or a small standing plaster should be placed on it.
As we said about the thumb, examine both during and after reduction of the swelling with what is appropriate, if anything like this occurs as described.
Fourteenth Chapter.
In the Fixation of Fractures of the Thigh.
The thigh bone is often fractured and can be felt because it moves forward and backward. Its fixation should be done by tying a bandage above the fracture and another below the fracture, with the patient lying on his stomach.
Then, each bandage should be extended to its side with an equal tension if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. If it is close to the upper part of the thigh, it should be fixed with a softer bandage made of wool or something similar.
At the upper part of the thigh near the groin, the bandage should be extended upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture. If the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be placed near the knee to extend downwards. Then, the doctor should align the bone with both hands until it is restored to its natural shape.
The bone will unite well. Then, the bandage and ligature should be applied if the limb does not develop a hot swelling. If a hot swelling does occur, leave it for some days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to treating it.
As for tightening it, it should be folded on the fracture with a wide stiff cap two or three times and what remains should be left. Then, fold the leg so that the heel is at the root of the ligament and insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below. The ends of the thread should be from above from both sides.
Then, wrap what remains of the cap around the leg and thigh. Place the splints on the thigh at the same location as the fracture, and put one splint on the shin bone. Fill the gap between the thigh and the leg with soft cloth to equalize the tension.
Then begin with strong tightening from the middle at the location of the fracture three or four times. The tighter you pull the bandage away from the location of the fracture, the less tight and softer it should be.
Then, address the ends of the thread you inserted between the thigh and the leg. Bind these with the splints above, then pass the ends of the thread downward until you reach the heel. Tighten them also with the other ends of the splints from the other side.
To ensure the bandage does not shift from its place, then leave the tension on it as long as the member shows no signs of necrosis, swelling, or distension. If anything like that occurs, address it promptly and correct whatever happens as we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a shard in the bone that causes pain, it should be smoothed if possible. If not, cut it out and extract it, treating the wound as described earlier until it heals. This fracture of the thigh may tighten without needing additional splints on the leg.
As we mentioned in the arm, except that its reduction does not expose the patient to a limp if reduced alone without being associated with the leg, it is necessary for its owner to be lame forever.
Know that the thigh strengthens in fifty days or a little more or a little less; all of this depends on the difference in temperaments and other conditions.
Fifteenth Chapter.
On Fracture of the Patella.
The patella seldom suffers a fracture, although it can be broken frequently. If a fracture does occur, it is either a crack or a break into its parts, whether accompanied by a wound or not.
And you can determine all of this by touch. To reduce it, align what has separated in its parts with the fingers until it comes together and heals according to how well alignment, gentle handling, and skill are achieved. Then apply a bandage and, if necessary, a circular splint.
And tighten the bandage above the appropriate tightness. Then manage all the conditions we described for other fractures, such as hot swelling, by counteracting each symptom with what is suitable until it heals.
Sixteenth Chapter.
On Reduction of Fracture of the Leg.
The leg has two bones: one is thick and is called the shinbone, and the other is thin and is called the tibia. Both are subject to types of fractures similar to those of the upper arm bones. Therefore, its reduction is the same as that of the arm, and the work is one. If both bones are broken, the leg turns in all directions. If only the finer bone is broken, the leg turns forward. And if only the thicker bone is broken and this happens lower down, this is something you are not unaware of.
Apply stretching and straightening and bind the fractures according to that, whether the break of the leg is a severe one with many shards. In such cases, the stretching should be less intense and you should be more gentle in its reduction.
And in the leg, there is something additional to the arm, which is that if you straighten the fractures and have finished all your work, take two fist-sized pieces of pine wood used for leveling rooms placed between the cracks of board joints or made from date palm leaves or similar materials.
Choose from it what is slightly thick, and let it not be thin strips. Let the length of each be equal to the length of the leg from the knee downwards. Then wrap each with two layers of cloth along its length, placing one strip on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let it extend from the knee to the foot, then tie the fṣqītin in three places at the ends and the middle. With this bandage, it will prevent the leg from leaning left or right and will immobilize it well. A wooden splint can also be used along the length of the leg.
It should be placed to preserve it from movement. You should do this more often if the fracture is accompanied by a specific wound, especially. Then inspect the leg every day and attend to it with great care for any swelling or puffiness or other such conditions. If such a thing happens, deal with it as should be done for recovery, and the broken thigh bone will heal in thirty days or so.
Seventeenth Chapter.
In Broken Bones of the Leg and Fingers.
As for the heel, it never breaks at all. As for the bones of the leg, they may break, and the fingers also rarely break; but sprains occur most often in them.
If the bones of the leg break and you see that some of these bones have approached each other, let the patient place his foot on the ground with it upright as when walking. Then rise and place your foot where those bones have raised, and press on them and straighten them.
Until it returns to its place. Then take the bandage and the pressure pad, place a small board under the sole of the foot, which has two flat ends, then tightly bind the foot with it after wrapping it in cloth and whatever else you need.
If three or four days have passed, release the bandage. You will find the bones either in their proper alignment if they were broken, or dislocated; proceed accordingly. As for if some fingers are broken, set and straighten them as described to you for setting the hand fingers.
Then make a splint for the broken finger along its length, and let it be slightly wider than the finger. Place this board under the foot as described, and bind it tightly. If the bones that broke are two or three or more, do so accordingly.
Make a splint of the width of one degree on a soft cloth for each finger and secure it at the bottom of the foot. Let each end of the splint protrude slightly from under the sole of the foot to hold securely.
You should not forget to observe all that we have mentioned for other fractures, dealing with each symptom as appropriate.
The Eighteenth Chapter.
In the Fracture of the Pubic Bone and the Symphysis Pubis in Women and Men.
When the vulva of a woman is fractured, seat her and lean her slightly backward. She should be supported from behind. Then, the midwife should pack the vulva with cotton until it is fully filled and becomes spherical in her vulva.
Then, shake the woman gently and gradually raise her spine slightly. As a result, the cotton will come out until it is at the entrance of the vulva, resembling a ball. This will help复原骨折的骨头。然后在她背上放置敷料，如果她想要小便，轻轻地取出棉花，让她小便。
Then, replace it as you first inserted it. Return to her original dressing. Do this for seven days or so, as it will heal. If you prefer, you can use a sheep's bladder; tie a tube at its mouth and insert the entire bladder into her vulva.
Then, blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder inflates inside the vulva. This will help复原骨折的骨头。然后用棉花填满，按照我们描述的方法保持几天直至痊愈。至于男人或女人的耻骨骨折，则需要用适当的治疗方法复位。
And I have adjusted it according to what we described in the bone of the hip, and it is not hidden from you the correctness in these rare fractures which occur seldomly, especially for one who has some experience and understands this book indeed, because the fracture itself guides you in most cases.
On the correct method of setting and binding it, understand well. As for the case of a man whose thigh bone is fractured, mention him briefly: you enter the genital area with an ass's weight, then put a bandage on it and bind it and leave it for three days or about that time until it heals.
Nineteenth Chapter.
On the setting of bone fractures accompanied by a wound.
It is advisable for one who is presented with a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or upper arm or similar, to set it immediately from its proper time if the conditions for setting are present as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound is bleeding, it is advisable to treat it immediately by applying a poultice of powder if you have nothing else at hand. Then proceed with setting the fracture on that same day without delaying.
If no hot swelling occurs, then if a hot swelling does occur, delay setting it until the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides. Do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, as it has exposed him to bad symptoms.
If the broken bone is exposed through the skin, it is advisable to try to reduce and adjust it gently with your hands. If you are not able to reduce and adjust it with your hands, do so using this instrument, which is made of iron, about seven or eight fingers in length. Expose it according to the size of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should take from it three or four, according to what he needs for treatment in each kind of fracture. Let it be a little thin in thickness so that it does not bend when pricked during the time of work.
And let its tip be sharp, with a slight bend at its tip. Its upper part should be slightly thick, while the lower half should be very thin. This is its shape:.
And it is called in Greek Perim, meaning a small nail. It should be made with its sharp, curved tip touching the end of the displaced bone, and it should be driven in once. When the bone returns to its position and some alignment is achieved, remove it and align the ends of the fracture with each other.
If its broken tip is thin and the machine did not grip it well, it should be cut at that tip so that the machine can grip it properly. If you are unable to reduce the bone as described at all, cut it as similar to the cuts we have mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the levers as much as possible. Then, remove any roughness and thin layers from the bone. If you reduce the bone and find that the patient experiences severe pain after reduction, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to reduce it to its natural position, do so, for this will greatly benefit the patient. Once your reduction is complete, immerse a cloth in a contracting black and special decoction; especially if it is summer. Do not apply any oil or ointment to the wound.
To prevent decay and corruption, then use the splints while you are finishing the bone setting and leave the wound open by making a hole in the bandages with scissors according to the size of the wound.
Be very careful not to tie the wound with the fracture, for many unskilled doctors have done this and caused either death or infection or swelling in their patients. Your tying should be soft and relaxed, different from the tying of other fractures.
If the wound is poor or is a large wound and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he finds pain in the place troubling, then you should not put splints on it. Instead, make bandages from strong torn cloth in the place of the splints and tie him with them.
If after one or two days you see that the wound has begun to produce pus, remove the bandage that was placed on it with moistening. Then use the fomentations and ointments which we are accustomed to apply to wounds, such as the quadruple ointment and similar ones. You should loosen the bandages.
You should inspect the wound in the evening and morning every day until it heals and recovers. It is necessary that the limb be positioned so that the pus flows downward easily. If days pass and the wound does not heal, and the pus continues to drain, know that there are small fragments of bone present.
You should examine the wound with a probe; what was from those fragments detached, remove and extract it. And what remained unremoved, if it causes the limb to decay and pain, burn it in pieces and remove it completely as you can.
If the wound shows signs of congestion or putrefaction or another type of corruption and contamination, it should be met with what corresponds to each sign from the treatment described in its respective section.
And you should pay attention to my words and understand them: if a large bone, such as the thigh bone or the humerus and similar large bones, is fractured and there is displacement in the limb, do not attempt to pull it or extract it. For often, such displacement leads to death. Rather, leave it until it decomposes; perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment. Otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty.
In the treatment of congestion that occurs following some fractures.
This congestion often occurs following the healing of fractures, especially those near joints. It can distort the shape of the limb and may even prevent the limb from performing its natural functions.
Examine it; if the congestion is soft, use remedies that constrict, such as saffron, frankincense, bitter substances, henbane, and acanthus, and so on. You may take some of these or all of them and mix them with a constricting decoction, or with egg white, or with vinegar.
And apply it to the congestion with difficulty and tighten it securely. Leave the tightening for many days without loosening it, then loosen it and repeat with another one until the congestion goes away by God's will, or secure a sheet of well-forged lead tightly over it.
If the mercury has a special property that removes whatever has formed in the organs, if the complication has hardened and intensified and necessity has urged removal of it, then make an incision from above and remove the excretions or strip them with some instruments until it goes away, and treat the wound until it heals.
The Twenty-First Chapter.
In treating fractures, if the bone heals but the limb remains soft and different from its original state.
If a bone fracture heals but the limb remains soft and weak, this is due to many reasons. One of the reasons is either excessive relaxation of the ligaments and re-tying them improperly, or excessive tightening of the ligaments to the point where nutrition cannot flow to the limb, or excessive and extreme fomentation, or excessive movements at improper times, or lack of blood in the patient's body and his weakness.
Treatment involves nourishing the patient and enriching his body to increase the blood in it, using steam baths, introducing joy and happiness, and the like. Then, applying fomentation to the limb to draw much nutrition onto it, or continuously fomenting it with lukewarm water. Until nutrition flows into it and returns it to its natural shape.
The Twenty-Second Chapter.
In treating fractured bones that heal crooked and prevent them from functioning as they should.
When a member that has been set after it healed shows distortion or displacement of the broken bone, or complications and deformation occurred for which reason the appearance of the member was spoiled. Except when the member does not refrain from its natural function, it should not be necessary to accept the claim that one can break a member even if it had healed, as this was often done by many ignorant physicians and bone-setters in our country.
And this action is severely blamed, leading to great danger, made easier by the physician. But if the distortion and complication are recent, it should be treated with water in which mild herbs have been boiled, such as palm-leaf, root, and crown-of-thorns, and similar substances.
And it is dressed with mild poultices such as well-made diaxilon or one takes the saliva of the root of the palm-leaf, mixes it with chicken fat and shiraj ointment, and dresses it with this. Or one takes juicy grapes, grinds them with guava residue and similar medications known as emetics.
And the complication will not resolve without constant gentle massage with hands, using the movement of the member in all directions at all times. If the distortion has become severe and hardened, and it is necessary to treat it with iron.
It should be cut above, releasing the connection of the bone and cutting what remains of the complication or bone with gentle cuts. Great care and gentleness must be used in this. Then, treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is the displacement of a joint from its proper place, hindering movement and deforming the organ. It causes severe pain and distress to the patient. Whenever this occurs, it should be addressed immediately to return it to its proper position.
It should not be delayed at all, for if it is delayed, the area will swell with a bruise that makes it harder to reduce the jaw. Therefore, it should not be delayed, moved, or extended when it is swollen, as it often causes spasms and painful distress to the patient.
But if this occurs, it should be addressed by first reducing the swelling. Then, it should be left until the bruise subsides slightly. After that, the organ should be treated with warm water and oil. Finally, it should be gently reduced, and each part should be treated as described in its respective section.
And I have also arranged the chapters on the jaw in accordance with what was mentioned for fractures, from the top of the body to the bottom.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
In the Treatment of the Lower Jaw.
The jawbones脱落仅在罕见的情况下，脱位有两种情况：要么轻微移位而略有松弛，要么完全脱位而彻底松弛。 Down to the vicinity of the chest, causing the patient to salivate excessively and unable to control it, and he cannot close his jaw, with his tongue jumbling when speaking. If the dislocation is slight, it often returns by itself with minimal intervention.
And if the dislocation is complete, it should be reduced with speed and not be delayed at all; this being that a servant holds the patient's head while the physician inserts his single finger at the root of the jaw inside his mouth if the jaw is on one side.
Or he inserts both his fingers at the root of the jaw if it is dislocated on both sides, and his other fingers from outside align it by doing so. He should order the patient to relax his jaw and let it go to every direction. The physician then aligns the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to reduce them, especially if both jaws are involved, apply a cloth soaked in hot water and ointment until they become easier to reduce. Do not delay their reduction at all as we have said. If they return and align, and the patient's mouth closes without relaxing, then at that time place on them sutures with quirti prepared from wax and ointment.
Then tie them gently with a relaxed bandage, and let the patient lie on his back with his head elevated between two pillows to prevent him from moving right or left. He should not be made to chew anything; instead, provide him with soft, easy-to-eat food.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is healed, let him eat as he pleases, and use it gently. Do not force him to open his mouth wide when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is fully healed and recovered.
If it is difficult to reduce the jaws when they remain dislocated for some time without returning to their places, this often results in fevers and constant headaches. The patient's stomach may become distended, and he may vomit bitter fluids repeatedly. If you see these conditions, know that there is a complication.
Often it is fatal within ten days.
The Twenty-Fifth Chapter.
For the Reduction of the Hyoid Bone and Humeral Head.
As for the trachea, it is not detached from the inner side by its connection to the chest. It may, however, be detached outwardly, and this can be perceived by touch. Its reduction involves having the patient lie on his back and extending his arms.
Then press the area with your hand firmly; it will return to place. Place a bandage and padding over it and secure it. As for its end that follows the humerus and is connected to it, it does not dislocate except rarely. If it does dislocate at any time, it should be reduced and aligned as we have mentioned and as you are capable of doing.
Then place a bandage and padding over it, secure it with a bandage, and order the patient to remain quiet and still until he recovers. By this same treatment, you can reduce the humeral head if it also has displaced from its position.
The Twenty-Sixth Chapter.
For the Reduction of the Humeral Head.
Know that the humeral head can dislocate in three ways: one, it may dislocate towards the elbow downward; two, it may dislocate towards the chest area; and perhaps it may dislocate above the humeral head, which occurs rarely.
It does not dislocate towards the back due to the position of the shoulder, nor towards the front near the nerve. It most often dislocates and comes out downward towards the elbow, especially in those with little muscle mass, as it comes out quickly and goes back in quickly.
As for those with much flesh, it is the opposite; that is, he will exit with difficulty and enter with difficulty. Sometimes a blow or fall may cause swelling of the biceps and burning inflammation, so one should examine this to ascertain its condition.
Then advance in treating him, and the jaw will be recognized if it is directed downward toward the wrist. If you join the dislocated bicep to the healthy bicep, you will find a clear discrepancy between them and find that the head of the bicep is displaced. Upon touching below the elbow, you will find the head of the bicep like an egg.
And the afflicted cannot raise his hand to his ear nor move it through all its motions. Similarly, if it dislocates toward the chest or above, you will find this evident upon touch, not hidden.
And this jaw can be easily reduced if it is soft or if the afflicted is a child. To reduce it, have him raise his hand above, then place your thumbs under his elbow and lift the joint forcefully upward to its proper position while the servant raises his hand and extends it above.
Then lower it slowly; it will return quickly. If it does not return as mentioned, and the dislocation is recent having occurred in many days, the afflicted should take a bath in hot water and use something relaxing and softening like boiling horseradish, cress, and crown flower in the water.
Then, the afflicted should lie on his back with a ball of wool placed under his elbow, which is moderate in firmness between soft and hard. Then, the doctor should place his heel on the ball and grasp the afflicted's hand with his hand and lower it downward.
If he kicks the ball, it will return to its place. If you wish to reverse its direction, do this: bring a man taller than the patient and stop him from one side, placing his elbow under the armpit of the patient and lifting his arm so that the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant pulls the patient's hand downward to his abdomen. If the patient is light, it should be weighted down with something else; then it will return its jaw from its place, and it may also return in another direction.
It should be focused on the ground a long plank, having its end round like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin. Then place it under the patient's arm pit after putting a soft cut on its end. The patient should stand along the length of the plank.
Then extend his hand downward from the other side and also extend his body from the other side with force. The joint will return to its place quickly. If it is difficult to return it as described, use this treatment: take a plank about two arms' length in size. Its width should be four fingers, and its thickness two fingers. It should have a round end to make it easier to enter the depth of the armpit as shown.
Then tie a soft cut on the round end to prevent the plank from harming the patient. Place it under the armpit, extend the entire hand and arm onto the plank downward, and tie the plank to the back, elbow, and hand.
Then place the arm on a bar. Slide it width-wise and extend the hand downward. Leave the rest of the body suspended from the other side. The joint will slip into its place. If it enters in any position possible, place a moderate-sized ball of wool under the armpit.
Then the prepared bandage with flour dust and egg white is applied to the entire thigh as if from above. Then the ball is tightly tied under the elbow and is wrapped with the bandage from above, and his hand is attached to his neck.
He should leave his hand unused for seven days, and it is necessary to make the patient's food light until the limb strengthens; this is quicker for its healing. Then it can be released after seven or five days, and he should be trained with movement. If it has stabilized and does not relax, then it is healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to moisture or another affliction, it should be treated with cauterization using three sesamum seeds as described in the chapter on cauterization. If this is done and the bandage is relaxed after seven days, but the joint does not stabilize.
And if you have applied the bandage and tightened it several times, but it does not stabilize and falls, relaxing and cannot be lifted above, then know that the nerve at the head of the thigh has either snapped or elongated or relaxed. Thus, know that the joint will never remain in its place.
And as for the jaw that is near the chest and breast and to its back, it should be reduced by pushing and stretching with the hands until it returns. Various tightening and treatment methods should be applied until it heals. If a mass appears in the limb after healing and the movement becomes slower.
The patient should take frequent baths until the mass softens and returns to its original state.
The Twenty-Seventh Chapter.
In the Treatment of Dislocation of the Elbow.
Dislocation of the elbow occurs with difficulty and can be reduced with difficulty as well. It dislocates in all directions, particularly forward or backward. The dislocation of the elbow is not hidden from you because it lies beneath the sight and touch, whatever form the dislocation takes.
And when you compare the dislocated elbow with the normal one, you will recognize it clearly by examining the joint. The arm cannot be bent, and the elbow cannot touch its armpit. It is advisable to act promptly and reduce the dislocation before a hot swelling appears.
For if a hot swelling appears, it is difficult to reduce it, and it may never be completely cured, especially if the dislocation is backward. This is the worst type of dislocation and the most painful. Death often accompanies such cases.
To reduce it, if it can be reduced, have an assistant extend his hands with both palms up and his arm stretched out. The physician's hands should be placed from above the elbow and below it, pushing the joint with both thumbs or the roots of his hands until it returns to its proper place.
If the dislocation is forward, it may be reduced by bending the arm once, striking it with the root of the palm that aligns with the elbow. If the dislocation does not return, use strong traction. This involves stretching the arm forcefully with two assistants.
The patient should be held by two assistants to prevent slipping during the traction. Then, wrap the arm in a folded cloth or a wide cloth and turn it in all directions. When the physician grasps the joint with his hands, rub it with oil to facilitate the reduction of the dislocation easily.
Then the joint is dislocated forcefully until it returns, and upon its return, a bandage containing compression and drying with the white of an egg should be applied, and it should be tightened firmly. The arm should be suspended from the collarbone and left for days.
Then it is resolved. If the joint remains in its place, release the ligament and leave it alone. If you see that the joint has not tightened sufficiently, reapply the bandage and ligament and leave it for days until it tightens, then resolve it. If a swelling occurs after the return of the joint and its resolution, let it be.
And if there is difficulty in movement, let the application be in the bath and gentle massage and cupping until it softens. Or make a lever with a fatty sheep and bind it to the joint, leaving it there for a day and a night, then remove it and put him in the bath. If he sweats, rub the joint with moderate rubbing.
Then reapply the lever once, twice, and thrice with entering the bath until it softens. If you wish to put a moistened ox hide on him with oil, do that repeatedly; for it will soften and return to its original state.
The Twenty-Eighth Section.
In the Treatment of Wrist Dislocation.
The wrist of the hand is often dislocated, and reducing its fracture is easier than that of other joints. Nevertheless, it should be reduced at the hour it dislocates before it becomes swollen or hot.
Fix his jaw by placing the patient's wrist on a board and extend a servant's hand and place the doctor's hand on the back of the joint and push it until it returns, unless one should look whether the jaw has dislocated to the inner side of the hand.
Let the patient place the back of his hand on the board during extension and flexion. If the jaw is protruding to the outer side of the hand, let him place the inner part of his hand on the board so that the doctor's hand can be placed at the same joint. If it returns to its place, otherwise bind it with a soothing compress for the swelling.
Leave it alone and do not attempt to reduce it again, for it cannot be endured and you will not be able to reduce it after a few days unless the joint remains in its place without causing any harm to the patient, except if the hand relaxes and is unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve has snapped or is detached, for there is no remedy except to bind it with a splint; it may help or may not help at all. Once the wrist is reduced, apply the splint as described, then bind it and leave it alone for five days.
Then exercise and train the hand. If its movement is hindered or there is any stiffness in it, soften it with hot water and massage several times until it becomes flexible.
The Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
On the Treatment of Jaws of Fingers.
If the fingers dislocate in all directions, and if one of them dislocates to the outer side or inner side of the hand, extend the finger and push the jaw with your thumb until it returns. Then bind the end of the finger and suspend it towards the direction it dislocated to.
Leave it for two days then release and stretch it until it becomes straight. If at night you find it as such, let her walk during the day and exercise her with movement and tie her at night. Continue doing this for days until she strengthens.
Do the same with it if it breaks towards the palm of the hand; tie it in about the same side and do with it as you did first until it heals. Do the same with it whenever it breaks towards any other direction.
The Thirtieth Chapter.
In the Treatment of Dislocating Beads of the Back.
If a bead of the back or neck dislocates completely, or if many beads have shifted from their places, there is no treatment for it, as death will speed up towards the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's feces are expelled without intent, as he cannot retain them. Often, some of his limbs relax, either both his legs and arms or one of them. If only one bead shifts from its place, it often disappears on its own.
And its disappearance can occur in four directions: that which disappears towards the back is called "hudda" (protrusion). As for its treatment, you should examine it. If the protrusion occurred in childhood, there is no treatment and no cure for it at all. However, if it occurred due to a fall or a blow, etc., then treatment may be applicable.
And I have mentioned various methods of treatment with long descriptions that do not yield much benefit. I have simplified what was said, so that a little explanation suffices instead of much of what they provided, and I have illustrated the apparatus differently from how they explained and described it.
I say that the curvature occurring in front in the chest has no remedy and cannot be cured. Likewise, this is so for that occurring on both sides. Only that which occurs specifically in the back can be treated with what I am describing, which is to extend the affected person in his position.
Place a level dock near the wall and stretch a wet thatch underneath it so as not to hurt his chest. Then place a vertical board planted in a hole in the ground at one end of the dock towards his head, and another board towards his feet at the other end of the dock.
And a servant holds the board, and let it not be secured in the hole. Another servant holds the other board in that manner. Then wrap around the affected person's chest and under their armpits with a soft tight cloth, and extend the end of the cloth to the board near his head and tie him there.
Then tie another cloth above his knees and above his ankles, and at the level of his armpits. Gather all the straps and tie them to the other board near his feet. Then each servant stretches the board towards their side with the strap, ensuring that neither board moves from its fixed position.
Except that they are not secured as we said. The physician places his hands on the peg with force until it returns, or he places a board on it. Then he leans his feet on the board until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, take a board whose length is about three cubits.
And a hole is dug in the wall near the location we said should be for the affected person. The end of the board enters this hole. Then place the middle of the board on the curvature, and place the physician's feet on the other end. He should tighten it well until he presses the peg.
Return it to its place, and if you wish to make it with the tube that is spun by hand, it is that you plant in the ground at the head of the ill one at the end of the couch two pieces of wood whose length is a cubit, and at his feet also in the end of the couch likewise two pieces of wood.
Let there be a span between each piece of wood, and make a hole in each piece through which the tube passes. Secure all the pieces of wood to the ground tightly enough so that they do not move at all. Insert a round rod, which is the tube, into which the bandage is coiled in the holes of the two pieces of wood.
At one end of it, make a hole in which you secure a rod a span long to coil the bandage. Do the same in the other piece of wood. Then, tighten the bandage that you have tightened around the chest of the ill one on the tube near his head, and the bandages that you have tightened around his legs on the tube near his feet.
Then, stand by each tube a servant who spins it with his hand using the spindle with which the tube is coiled. The doctor straightens out the bump as we have mentioned before. This is a depiction of the tube, the couch, and the ill one.
Then, after the vertebrae have returned to their place and the area is straightened, it should be covered with a dried bandage soaked in egg white, then pressed. Then, place over the bandage a splint made of wood whose width is three fingers or so, and whose length is sufficient to cover the bump.
And keep some correct beads, then bind it with the bandage that is appropriate. The ill one should use gentle food until he recovers. If some knots remain in the area after full recovery, it should be treated with medicines that relax and soften, along with the use of the splint we described for a long time.
And for this, a lead plate may be used. And there may appear a notch at the end of the vertebrae, and one may think it is dislodgement, and it would be an extra bone that has notched. Do not treat it with this remedy, for perhaps death may occur.
The Thirty-First Chapter.
In the Treatment of Dislocated Hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are dislocated only by displacement, not by a slight separation or subluxation, as is the case with other joints. The hip joint can be dislocated in four directions.
And this is because it can dislocate inward, outward, forward, and backward. It dislocates more frequently inward, and less often forward or backward. The sign of its displacement inward is that if you compare the healthy thigh of the unaffected side with the affected side, the affected side appears longer.
The knee will be more extended than the healthy side. The affected thigh cannot bend at the waist, and the area below the waist will appear swollen with a distinct swelling before the femur head has shifted there.
The sign of dislocation outward is the opposite of these symptoms. The sign of dislocation forward is that he will fully extend his leg, but cannot bend it without pain, and there will be no swelling in the knee. If he tries to walk, he will not be able to do so forward.
He will retain his urine and his waist will appear swollen. When walking, his step will be on the heel. The sign of dislocation backward is that he cannot straighten his knee or bend it before bending the waist, and his affected leg will also be shorter than the other.
The Arabic is relaxed and the position of the thigh bone at the location of the thigh is clear. As for refuting this type of fracture, you should look and see if the fracture is old, having remained in its place without attempt to reduce it and has remained in that state; there is no remedy for it at all.
It should not be treated if the fracture is new. As for one that has been fractured recently, whether it is of any kind from the four-sided fractures, you should promptly twist the joint and extend it inward and outward, and move it left and right; perhaps.
It returned and did not require any other treatment. If it does not return in this manner, you should use a strong assistant to extend his leg from below either with your hands or with a strap tied above the knee on his leg. Another assistant should extend him from above.
By inserting his hands under his armpits, then tying a soft strap at the root of the thigh and holding the end of the strap with a third assistant. The extension should be either from in front towards the clavicle or from behind towards the back.
They should extend him all at once so that the injured part rises with his body from the ground and remains suspended. This type of extension is common for all four kinds. If the fracture returns as we said, then no special treatment is necessary. Otherwise, each kind requires the specific treatment I have mentioned.
As for its specific reduction if the fracture is inward, the injured should lie on his healthy side, then place a strap at the root of the thigh between the thigh bone and the position below the armpit.
Then the ligature is extended upwards from the Arabian side to the upper part of the body towards the clavicle. Then another strong assistant supports it with his arms and holds the thick part of the delicate thigh tightly, extending it strongly outward; for otherwise, it will return to its place.
This kind is easier than other types of treatment by which this part can be restored. If you find it difficult and it does not respond to this type of treatment at all, it will be necessary to tie the legs of the delicate one firmly with a strong, flexible ligature on the ankles and the knees.
It should be four fingers' breadth from each of its neighbors, and the delicate leg should be extended more than the other by about two fingers' breadth. Then the delicate one is suspended from the head by a beam in the house, and there should be a distance of about an arm's length from the ground.
Then you order a strong boy to hold the upper part of the thigh and another boy hangs the delicate one, pushing the boy holding the thigh with force. For the joint will return to its place quickly, but if it is displaced outward, it will be复原。请注意，翻译已结束。
Then the delicate one should lie on the bench as we described for the convex case, and the ligature should be tightened particularly around his delicate leg and chest. Then two beams are placed under his legs and head, respectively. Finally, a additional beam is placed in the middle of the bench and securely fixed.
It should be wrapped with a wet cloth to prevent injuring the delicate one. Place the beam between his thighs so that it does not slip downward during extension. Then each assistant extends towards their side, and the doctor adjusts the hip with his hands; if it responds by returning.
And otherwise, place the board upon it and cover it as we mentioned in convexity the same. Except that it should be that the affected lie on his correct side. And if the dislocation is to the front, it should be lengthened by one time and he should remain in this description exactly on the deck.
The doctor places the掌侧 of his right hand on the affected iliac bone and presses it with the other hand, making the pressure extend downward to the knee region. If the dislocation is to the rear, it should not be lengthened downward.
It is elevated from the ground. It should be placed on something solid. Also, it should be abducted to the side as we mentioned when he was lying on his side with the ligaments tightened.
As we said before, it should also be compressed with the board at the site where the joint has dislocated. When the hip joint returns to its proper place, you will not fail to notice the sign of its return, which is that the affected side leg can be lengthened.
When you see them level and the affected one can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned to its proper place. Then compare the thighs and apply the bandage, tightening it with a cloth so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The affected one must remain at rest for three or four days. Then remove the ligaments and bandage, and compare the legs with each other. If you find them equal in length, know that the joint has stabilized. Then release the affected one to walk. If you see any signs of relaxation, proceed with caution.
Rejoin and compress it according to your initial strength, and leave it also for three days. Then dissolve it and let him walk on it slowly for several days until it strengthens indeed if Allah wills.
The Second and Third Chapter.
In the Treatment of Dislocation of the Knee.
The knee dislocates on three sides: to the inside, to the outside, and downwards (behind). It never dislocates forwards. The sign of its dislocation is to order the patient to hug his leg to his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated. To reduce all sides of its dislocation, order the patient to sit leaning forward with his legs stretched out if he can do so. Then seat a servant behind him to hold his midsection and bend him slightly backward. You should then sit on his thighs.
And place your back against his face, and position his leg between your legs. Then hold his knee with both your hands and interlace your fingers around his knee. With great strength, press the sides of his knee with your palms. Meanwhile, another servant should stretch his leg until the knee returns to its proper place.
And the sign that it has returned is that the leg adheres to the thigh willingly and comfortably. Then dress it, adhere the leg to the thigh, and bind them all together with a bandage for three or four days. Then release them. Use only light walking for several days until it strengthens.
If you are unable to reduce it as described, then use the strong stretching with the bandages I have described for treating the hip until it returns.
The Thirty-Third Chapter.
In the Treatment of Dislocating the Heel.
The heel may dislocate partially and may be reduced completely. Its reduction may be either inward or outward. The sign of its reduction is that you will see the heel swollen and protruding towards the side to which it was reduced.
As for the treatment of its partial dislocation, it is easy to reduce it by gently stretching with the hands and aligning it until it returns to place. As for its complete dislocation, it should be seated in a sitting position, and a strong servant should hold him from behind, supporting him in the middle.
Then hold his foot with your right hand from above and with your left hand from below at the ankle. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand two times. Stretch it with your left hand and push the chest of the foot towards the leg gently with your right hand.
Do this twice as we have described. In the third time, push the chest of the foot towards the leg while pulling the ankle. If it returns in one or two attempts and you see the foot level, then continue. Otherwise, redo the process; it will return to place.
If it does not return as we have described, then place the injured person on his back on the ground and drive in two stakes firmly into the ground to be placed between his thighs, and wrap him with a cloth to prevent any harm to the injured. Then a servant should secure his thighs.
Then another attendant stretches the limb either with his hands or with a belt that ties it around the ankle, then each attendant stretches differently from his companion and the prop is upright between my healthy thigh supporting him so that his body does not fall downward upon stretching, then the physician straightens the jaw with his hands.
And another attendant holds the healthy leg downward, for the jaw returns quickly. When the jaw returns and you see that its return is correct, carry the splint and difficulty and tie it tightly with belts and secure the foot with the belt downward. It is advisable to beware of the nerve that is above the heel from behind.
Lest the belt be too tight and hurt him, then leave it on for two or three days. If the belt relaxes, tighten it again, then release it on the third or fourth day. The injured limb should abstain from walking for forty days.
If he desires to walk before this period, it is not safe that the jaw may give way and become ineffective, nor will treatment be accepted thereafter. If a hot swelling appears, it is advisable to use what we have described for pain relief in another part of the treatment and application until it subsides.
The Fourth Thirty-Fourth Chapter.
In the Treatment of Jaw Dislocation of the Finger.
It should be straightened what has dislocated from it with a gentle stretch. This is not difficult but easy. If the jaw is in one of the bones at the back of the foot, it should be made that the injured limb's foot rests on a level surface of the ground.
Or on a board and standing like a walker, then stand you placing your foot where we mentioned from those joints, then press it with your foot firmly until it returns and you see that it has aligned and there is no displacement in the place.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot, taking the whole foot, and let it have two ends. Then tighten and secure it firmly for three days. Afterwards, loosen it and protect it from walking for many days until it strengthens and you may fear the return [of the dislocation] if Allah wills.
The Thirty-Fifth Chapter.
On Kinds of Jaw Injury That May Occur with a Wound or a Fracture or Both Together.
When any of this occurs and you treat it and set it, it often results in death.
For this reason, it should not be undertaken to treat such a case except by one who is skilled in the craft, experienced, gentle, patient, neither rash nor bold. And at first, only the medicines that calm hot swellings should be used, and the healthy should be allowed to recover on their own.
For this reason, O Lord, except what I hope will be safe from disease with a mild illness and where you have shown some hope in it, return him to me at the very beginning before the hot swelling occurs.
If the limb returns as desired, use the treatment that calms hot swellings and treat the wound with ointments suitable for it. If the jaw is broken and there are shards of bone protruding, extract them. And complied with that which we mentioned in the simple diseases as stated before regarding their locations.
And strive to the utmost and purify yourself from entering the way of risk as stated before. I have advised you of this, for it will benefit you in your efforts and make your situation easier with the will of Allah.
